FRENCH PREMIER COMBES RETIRES
AFTER A FINAL TRIUMPH.
Loubet Will Receive Cabinets Resignation on Wednesday
PARIS, Jan. 1 J.âCombes has retired from the Premiership with his face to the foe and in all prob
ability Rouvier will head the next French Ministry. The resignations of all members of the Cabinet
have been drawn up and will be presented to President Loubet on Wednesday. By retiring just
after he had won a final triumph over the opposition in the Chamber ot Deputies, Combes is left in
a position where he will have a voice in the selection of the new Cabinet and will wield a powerful
influence in shaping the future policy of the Government. His successor is expected to adhere
closely to his programme.
PARIS, Jan. 15.âThe ministr-- of
Combes, after emerging successfully
from its bitter midnight struggle in I
the Chamber of Deputies, has decided :
to abandon the field while some portion j
of its prestige still remains, and has
signified its intention of resigning. The
resignations will be presented on Wed
nesday mornng.
Friends of the Ministry maintain
that its retirement is voluntary, as
Combes has succeeded in resisting the
effort to compel his fall. The opposi
tion, however, hails the decision as a
victory, claiming that the small ma
jority for the Ministry last night left
Combes shorn of effective strength to
carry out his policies.
Combes called at the Elysee Palace
to-day to confer with President Loubet,
but the latter was overwhelmed by the
fatal illness of his mother, who died
to-day. The President's affliction pre
vented any discussion of the Cabinet
situation.
PREMIER LOOKS CAREWORN.
Premier Combes received the various
Ministers, during the day. He looked
careworn after ten hours of parliamen
tary battle, in which he occupied the
floor for three hours and was the center
of a continuous attack during the re
maining hours. He spoke freely of the
Intention of his Cabinet to retire as a
whole and outlined the details of its
closing work. He regards the retire
ment as wholly voluntary, as the votes
last night, although showing small ma
jorities, gave enough margin to permit
of the carrying on of the ministerial
programme. However, he considers
that his departure will facilitate the
realization of the programme, instead
of interrupting it, since his successor
will be able to unite the discordant
elements which have become personally
hostile to him.
It is considered that Combes' volun
tary withdrawal will permit him to ex
ercise a powerful and probably a de
cisive influence in the choice of his
successor, similar to that which Wal
deck-Rousseau exercised when volun
tarily relinquishing power.
To-night it was said that Rouvier ap
peared to have been practically dp- |
elded upon as the future chief of the |i
Ministry and that he had asked Del
casse and Berleaux to retain the For- \
eign and War portfolios, respectively.
The logic of the stiuation appears
to point to Rouvier. He is accept
able both to Combes and to those who
arc rebelling against his authority.
However, if Combes exercise the au
thority to name his own successor h<
may designate Brisson, who as Presi
dent of the Chamber of Deputies as
sisted in the execution of the Combes
programme. The names of MUlerand,
Poincere, Doumer and Clemenceau are
prominently mentioned, their strength
depending upon later combinations in
the majority groups. However, the per
sonal influence the President usually
exercised is obscured by death within
his household, making the outcome of
the reorganization of the Ministry in
creasingly doubtful.
NO CHANGE IN POLICY.
The effect of the change uoon the
Government's policies is not considered
to be great. The new Ministry will have
the same majority as that supporting
Combes. This majority has repeatedly
upheld Premier Combes' project tend-

RECEIVES
A FORTUNE
IN "TIPS"
Waiter Puts Awa_j*
$40,000 in Five
Years,
Special Dispatch to The Call.
DENVER, Jan. 15. âFrancois Du
mon, a Frenchman and a waiter, who
has cleared $40,000 in five years from
"tips," left Denver to-night for San
Francisco. He has heard much of the
coast metropolis and its resemblance
to Paris and he will visit it before go
ing home.
During the World's Fair Dumon was
a waiter in one of the most fashion
able cafes in St. Louis. He says he
cleared nearly $8000 0n "tips" last
year.
The young man speaks six lan- i
gua#?s. His father and grandfather
were waiters in Paris and he was
trained especially for the work, as, he
says, all the best waiters are. Dv- ;
mon intends to spend a life of ease
in Paris after seeing the coast. He
affects a silk hat and a Prince Albert
frock suit, but makes no effort in any
other way to appear the other than
what he is.
KILLS HIMSELF BECAUSE
OF PROLONGED STRIKE
Fall River Weaver Cuts His Throat
When Peace Negotiations
Fail.
FALL RIVER, Mass., Jan. 15.âTo
day added # its quota to the tragedies
associated with the cotton mill strike
here. John Neville, a weaver, who,
with his crippled wife, had suffered
much, after reading of the failure of
the strike conference at Boston yes
terday stood before a mirror and
drew a razor across his throat. He
died immediately.
Three striking loom fixers, James
Logan, Michael Waddington and Pe
ter Hudson, who since the mills closed,
have lodged together in one small
room, were found unconscious from il
luminating gas to-day and removed to
a hospital. They will recover.
Killed While Cleaning Pistol.
CHICAGO. Jan. 15.âWhile cleaning
a revolver to-day, which he supposed
was not loaded, Frank W. Welch, a
well-known attorney, was accidentally
shot and instantly killed.

ROSSEAU
CONFESSES
TO POLICE
Admits Attempt to
Blow Up Liner
UmMa.
Explosion at Statue of Fred
erick the Great Also
His Work.
Philadelphia Prisoner Is Classed as
''an American Fatriotic
'FanLtic"
PHILADELPHIA, Jan. 15. â"Gess-
ler Rosseau," who was arrested in this
city on Thursday with an unloaded in
fernal machine in his possession, ad
mitted to the police to-day that he is
the man who attempted to destroy the
statue of Frederick the Great In
Washington last Tuesday, and also
that it was he who sent the trunk con
taining an infernal machine to the
British steamship Umbria in New
York in May, 1903. The prisoner,
whose right name is not known, made
these admissions In the office of Cap
tain Donaghy of the Philadelphia de
tective force, after he had been iden
tified by persons brought here from
New York and Washington for that
purpose. He gives no reasons for the
attempted outrages, except that "there
are too many foreign affairs in this
country." Aft3r having Rosseau un
der tire all afternoon, the police
classed him as "an American patriotic
fanatic."
The prisoner gave no information
voluntarily to the police, he admitting
only those things which the police had
fastened upon him. Both the Wash
ington and New York authorities want
the man and it is probable that he
will be turned over to the police of
the latter city.
HOW ROSSEAU WAS CAUGHT.
The upprehension of Rosseau was
due to the disappearance of Owen
Kelly, a wealthy Irish-American, who
is prominent in Irish-American socie
ties in this country. Keliy disappeared
on October 25 and from time to time
the newspapers, particularly of the
East, published articles on the
mysterious disappearance. These, the
police believe, undoubtedly attracted
Rosseau's attention. On Thursday he
called on Patrick Kelly, the brother of
the missing man, and offered to take
him to his brother in New York for
$500. He said he and Owen Kelly be
longed to secret societies that were
organized for the purpose of blowing
up British ships in American ports.
The valise he carried, he said, con
tained explosives.
Patrick Kelly notified the police and
Rosseau was taken Into custody. The
valise contained an infernal machine
of the clock-arrangement variety, but
no explosives. About the machine was
wrapped a Washington newspaper.
This gave the police the clew that he
might" know something of the ex
plosion at the statue of Frederick the
Great and his picture was taken and
sent to both the Washington and the
New York authorities. In the mean
time he also was suspected of know
ing something of the steamship Um
bria incident.
To-day Captain Robert Boardman
of the Washington detective force and
Alfred Carter, colored, and George H.
Hazel, cabmeiv of Washington, who
saw the man who attempted to blow
up the statue, and Detective Sergeant
Arthur Carey of New York and a Mrs.
Curry, who keeps a bÂ°arding-house in
the latter city, arrived here for the
purpose of identifying the prisoner.
PRISONER IS IDENTIFIED.
When Rosseau was brought Into the
office of Captain Donaghy, Hazel said:
"1 guess you know me," to which the
prisoner replied, "How do you do?"
Rosseau bowed to Carter and then
greeted Mrs. Curry. Having satisfied
themselves beyond doubt that the
identification was complete, the police
officials of the three cities set at work
to obtaining a confession from the
prisoner.
In reply to a question he admitted
the attempted outrage on the statue.
He said he had manufactured the in
fernal machine himself and that it
consisted of a candle, fuse and "dyna
mite engine." Where he made it he
would not say. The failure to destroy
the statue, he said, must have been
due to the melting tallow spoiling the
fuse or interfering with the dynamite.
Rosseau then gave the police the in
teresting Information that he had in
tended marking a second attempt to
blow up the statue, this time with a
time-clock machine. As it was diffi
cult to get past the sentries after
nightfall, he intended going to the
arsenal at about dusk to arrange the
infernal machine so that it would ex
plode about midnight and then hang
it on the statue.
ATTEMPT TO BLOW 7 UP LINER.
Regarding the sending of the in
fernal machine to the steamship Um
bria, Rosseau said that he made part
of the Umbria Infernal machine in
Chicago and then took it to New York
in a trunk. He admitted having writ
ten the note to Police Commissioner
Greene, as follows:
"The Mafia greets you and wishes
you well. The society has declared
war against England and, has ordered
the destruction of every steamer fly
ing the British flag that sails out of
New York. The society has under
taken to clear New York harbor of
British ships, and it will succeed."
It was this note of warning that led
to the finding of the trunk before It
was placed on the steamship.
The prisoner was closely questioned
regarding the motive for blowing up
the Umbria, but was noncommittal.
He said that he was born in America
and that he was a farmer. He said he
EDUCATION
AND RACE
STERILITY
Stanley Hall Says
They Go Hand
In Hand,
Culture Not Transmitted
Through the Channel
o! Heredity.
Only a Â£mall Percentage of College
Graduates and Few
Rear Children.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BALTIMORE, Jan. 15.â"Any educa
tion that develops progressive sterility
or race suicide is a failure. At present
our higher education would soon depop
ulate the country if it became univer
sal. If there is any truth in the old
saw of Huxley that an ounce of hered
ity is worth a. ton of education, it fol
lows that if our academic culture
makes the supreme joy of parenthood
less ardently craved it is already bio
logically bankrupt.
"Thus,, as this evil is worse with
women, and as man unaided by her
can. never hope to solve the problem of
her sex, the argument for the very
higher education of the few best
women is that thus, somehow or some
wheae one may arise who will find out
a new and better way."
This statement was made by Presi
dent Stanley Hall of Clark University
in his address before the American As
sociation of Universities. "Co-Instruc
tion" was the topic of the session. In
his paper President Hall said:
"Perhaps the gravest danger is that
twenty years after graduation about
one-half of the college girls of three
women's colleges and one-quarter of
the graduates of nine Eastern colleges
for men are unmarried. Few marry
later than twenty years after gradua
tion and those who do marry have only
two children per marriage, or less than
3 per cent. If civilization is man's self
domestication and higher education is
its acme, why should not the test of
fertility also apply to It as well as to
domestication: and if our acadamic
youth and maidens -are God-chosen
ones, why should not the Abrahamic
promise apply to them if they keep
covenant with their own nature?
"Are we not losing our labor if the
educational classes are plowed under,
as at present, and culture is not trans
mitted into the most ancient form of
wealth and worthâheredity?"
SCIENTIST
WILL WAR
UPON FOG
Vapor Banks to Be
Snot Away With
Electricity.
Special Dispatch to Ttaa Call.
NEW YORK, Jan. 15. âAn attempt
will be made soon to dissipate fog in
New York harbor by shooting a 250,
--000 volt electric bolt through barbed
wire into the air. This is the method
which Sir Oliver Lodge has adopted
in his wonderfully successful fog dis
pelling experiments at Birmingham
University, England.
The experiments here probably will
be conducted off the Quarantine sta
tion on Staten Island, or at Governor's
Island, at both of which high masts
or poles will be available.
The fogs in New York harbor have
been unsually dense and prolonged
this winter.
Sir Oliver asserts that he can dis
sipate smoke, fog and mist. Inci
dentally, he produces rain. This he
accomplishes by the production of
continuous high potential electrical
discharges. He has effected a com
bination of high potential rectifiers.

CHADWICK
JEWELRY
LOCATED
Gems of Great Value
Are Traced by tlie
Government
Incomplete Search Reveals
Stones Worth Quarter
of a Million.
Said to Have Eeen Smuggled Over
and fhen Given as Presents
to Friends.
CLEVELAND, Jan. 15.âThe Plain
Dealer to-morrow will say: Investiga
tion into the charges that Mrs. Cassie
L. Chadwick smuggled large quantities
of diamonds, gems and other precious
stones into this country has developed
the fact that the woman dealt in pre
ciouÃ stones to an almost fabulous ex
tent. In order to make a case on the
charge of* smuggling it was found
necessary for the Government authori
ties to trace ail the deals for diamonds
and jewelry made by M*%. Chadwick,
both in this country and in Europe.
The statement has been made by Col
lector of Customs Leach that jewelry
and precious stones worth more than
$250,000 and handled by Mrs. Chadwick
have been traced and positively located.
It is added that this great amount of
jewelry has been uncovered with the
investigation still incomplete. Of this
amount the Government is directly in
terested in about one-third.
While the Government is attempting
simply to collect any unpaid duties on
diamonds brought from Europe, it is
understood that all the evidence se
cured will be placed at the disposal of
Receiver Nathan Loesser and that he
will be able to realize thousands of dol
lars for the creditors of the woman.
The investigation has led to the dis
covery that only a small portion of the
jewelry was put up as collateral for
loans made by Mrs.jChadwick 'and that
by far the greater portion went as gifts
to friends and acquaintances.
The regular customs rate of duty on
diamonds and Jewelry brought to this
country is 10 per cent of the value of
the goods. Figuring on this basis, the
Government authorities expect to real
ize between $8000 and $10,000. The rest
of the proceeds from the sale of any
jewelry on which duty has not been
paid will be turned over to Loesser to
be distributed among the Chadwick
creditors.
"It is simply the truth to say that
Mrs. Chadwick handled pearls, rubies,
sapphires, emeralds and garnets almost
literally by the peck," said a Govern
ment official. "The testimony of experts
shows that the woman did not handle
anything but the finest articles, and
there is scarcely a penny's worth of
cheap jewelry in the entire lot that has
been traced."
According to Government officials by
far the greater portion of the jewelry
is in the hands of innocent persons.
The work of informing these people
that their claim on the jewelry is to be
protested is going on.
It is believed that practically alj the
people who have the jewelry will agree
to a settlement without taking the mat
ter into the courts. At the same timÂ«
the Government authorities, as well as
Receiver Loesser, are taking steps to
enforce their demands matter if
so compelled.
SUFFERS RATHER
THAN ASK HELP
Scion of a Famous French
Family Lives in Want With
Plenty of Means at His Call
LOS ANGELES. Jan. 15.âComte
Vaillant de la Croix, the last male
survivor of one of the noblest families
of France, is dying penniless in Los
Angeles because he is too proud to let
his wealthy relatives in Paris know
aught of his sickness and poverty.
Since the days of the crusades the
house of De la Croix has been one of
prominence among French aristocracy.
The father of the present Count was
killed in the Franco-Prussian war.
Count "Vaillant chose music as his pro
fession and practically renounced his
title when he came to America about
fifteen years ago in pursuance of his
musical career. He was educated un
der the best masters of Paris and
Florence and under the stage name of
Signor Bravura he gained renown as
a leading barytone in Europe and
America.
In the East the Count achieved
some success as a manager of opera
companies. He was at various times
instructor in voice culture in the Bos
ton Conservatory of Music, the Knick
erbocker Conservatory of New York,
the Illinois Conservatory of Music and
other institutions. Several years ago
he came to the Pacific Coast for the
benefit of his health and opened a
studio in San Francisco. Mrs. Percy
Cartwright and Miss Jessie Baker of
Oakland were trained by him for
grand opeÂ»a. #
About a year ago he came to Los
Angeles, where he had a large class
of pupils. His health broke down four
months ago. His resources gave out
and he would now be in the County
Hospital but for the charity of
strangers. De la Croix has a wealthy
sister in Paris, but his pride will not
permit him to apply to her for â¢nist
> ance.

RESIGNATION
OF LOUBET'S

Continued From Page 1, Column 1.
ing toward separation of church and
state. It is expected therefore that the
programme relating to separation will
be carried out, although Combes' re

The issue which proved most decisive
in hastening the resignation of the

The enactment of an income tax and
other important measures will not be
materially affected.
Premier Combes was waited upon

of the Chamber of Deputies, who urged
that he reconsider his decision to re
sign, but he positively refused to do so.
NEW YORK'S IMMIGRATION
COMMISSIONER RESIGNS
Upon Relinquishing Office, Williams
Receives Letter of Appreciation
From Roosevelt.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 15.âWilliam
Williams, Commissioner of Immigra
tion at New York, has tendered and
the President has accepted his resig
nation to take effect February I'D. In
his letter dated January 12, accepting
Williams' resignation the President
says:
Most reluctantly I have come to the con
clusion that I shall have to accept, your res
ignation. In accepting It, let me repeat to
you In writing what I said to you by word of
mouth in endeavoring to get you not to insist
upon It. I feel that you have rendered a ser
vice of real and hlch importance to the whole
nation in your management of the office under
you. When I asked you to take the position
I realized that I was askings, you to do what
meant genuine self-sacrifice oh your part; and
I roaltze that your consenting to stay in so
lons has meant further loss to you. But
surely you mvist fed recompensed by the
k-> wledge of the value of your work. You
have set a standard of unceasing industry, of
untiring energy, of high administrate ability
and of single-minded devotion to duty, which
your successor will find it difficult to equal,
no matter how good a man he may be.
CLING TO THE FINERY
OF PALEFACE FRIENDS
Indians Marry According to Tribal
Custom, but Wear Wedding Gar
ments After Fashion of Whites.
RENO, Jan. 15. âAn educated young
couple of the Shoshone tribe, intent
upon following somewhat in the steps
of the white man, but disliking the
thought of entirely abandoning the
customs of their people, were married
in the presence of their people at Aus
tin yesterday. Indians from surround
ing camps were in attendance.
The ceremony was performed by
Chief Tom Toi Toi. The couple were
attired in the customary wedding garb
of their paleface friends. A wedding
supper was also served.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 16, 1905.
ATTACHES OF THE LEGISLATURE FULL
OF WOE BECAUSE WORK IS IN SIGHT
Economists Tafe Steps to (ompef Appointees to Attend
Early Morning Roll
Call Planned by
Reformers.
Special Dljrnatrh to The CSC
SACRAMENTO, , Jan, 15.âConsider
able excitement was manifested to- ]
night among the legislators when it
was announced that some day this ,
week, probably to-morrow, the minor- 1
ity in both Houses would demand a ;
roll call of attaches. The minority in '.
this instance is that division of each
House opposed to the patronage meas
ures as passed, and which held that '
for economy's sake the legislators
should reduce the patronage to the !
minimum, Bay of $10 for the Senate and
$6 for the Assembly. They lost their
fight and now they are determined that j
no matter how many were appointea
they must report lor duty and be at ;
least wining to labor should the neces
sity arise. :
The claim is broadly made that at
past sessions about one-hait ot the 1
attaches of both Houses did no other j
labor than to take the oath of office. 1
hypothecate their warrants and start i
for home to take up their usual occu- j
pations. Though there has been no evi
dence to date that this scheme will be i
repeated the minority says it will take
Steps to see that it is not. It will sec j
that every attache reports to the ser- |
geant-at-arms at the usual hour, and
tailing to do so will be reported to the
Chairman of the committee on attaches j
for removal.
The small excitement of to-nitrht is
due to the fact that many of the legis
lators do not know whether their favor
ite appointes are in town and fear that
should the roll call be demanded be
fore the word is well circulated it will
disclose an absence of employes of both
Houses that will discomfit even the
most rabid advocate of generous pat
ronage.
Quite a number of legislators and
lobbyists arrived from the bay cities on
the late trains to-night. Jere Burke
of the Southern Pacific was the most
prominent among the latter, and
though he confessed that there were
no more Senators to be elected, it was
said there was other work to do that
would require his presence here for a
few days.
The returning legislators experienced
conflicting emotions regarding the
minority scheme to demand a roll call
of all attaches, and the older heads,
though saying they had no obiection
to such a course, pointed out that be
fore the minority could carry through
its plan, which might possibly Â©m
bassass the majority, it would first have
to consult the majority and if the ma
jority should fail to see, things in the
light of the minority its storming
would avail it little.

SAYS RATE SUPERVISION
WILL AFFECT EMPLOYES
President Tuttle of Boston and Maine
Koad Talks of Roose
velt's Policy.
BOSTON, Jan. 15.â1n an address be
fore the Brotherhood of Locomotive
Engineers, Division 61, on the occasion
of )ts fortieth anniversary to-day, Pres
ident Lucius Tuttle of the Boston &
Maine Railroad, spoke of President
Roosevelt's proposal that railroad rates
be regulated under Congressional su
pervision and authority.
Tuttle declared that such a policy, if
carried out, must inevitably affect the
income of the 1,000,000 persons who de
rive their income directly from the
railroads. He did not think it had been
or could be shown that the makers of
the constitution ever had anjaÂ» other
thought in mind in connection with the
delegation to Congress of the power to
regulate interstate commerce relations
than a well-defined intent to forever
prevent the erection by any State of
any customs, tariffs or other barriers
that should be an obstruction to the
free currents of commerce.
"The railroads in 1903," he said,
"were able only to pay their share
holders an average dividend of less
than 3 per cent and any further general
reduction of rates must necessarily be
followed by a reduction of operating
expenses.'" â¢
GOVERNMENT INTERESTED
IN THE FOUNDLING CASES
United States District Attorney Takes
Hand in Habeas Corpus Pro
ceedings in Arizona.
PHOENIX. A. T., Jan. 15.âThere is
much curiosity concerning what will
transpire when the Suprema Court of
Arizona convenes to-morrow, it now
having on trial the habeas corpus pro
ceedings in the case of the foundlings
sent to Clifton, A. T., by the New York
Foundlings' Home, and left in Catholic
Mexican families, from whom they were

New York home.

Tuesday and besides the lawyers on
the two sides United States District
Attorney Nave has intervened by direc
tion of the United States Attorney
general.

WILL SEEK REVENGE.
Movement on Foot to Spoil Prescott's
Chance for Office.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 15. âA move
ment has been started in San Bernar
dino by prominent citizens of the
county to bring to the personal notice
of President Roosevelt the alleged lack
of honor which Speaker F. C. Prescott
displayed in ignoring the wishes of his
constituents in the matter of electing
a United States Senator. It is their
plan to send to the President a copy
of the resolutions of the county con
vention in which they declared for
Bard, and also copies of Prescott's
speeches before election in which he
stated that "It is not what Prescott
wants, but what you want."'
Prescott is looking for a Federal ap
pointment, so think the people, and it
is apparently their desire that he shall
be well known at Washington.

BLOW NEAR THE HEART
ENDS LIFE OF WOMAN
Nevada Business Man Strikes Mother
in-Law With Fist and Death
Results.
RENO, Jan. 15. âDan Regan, for
merly of Vacaville, Cal., a business
man of Sparks, went home in an in
toxicated condition early this morning
and began demolishing the furniture
in the house. His mother-in-law, Mrs.
Harriet Hess, tried to stop him and
he attacked her, first with a shovel
and then with his fists. He struck her
a blow near the heart and she died a
few moments later.
Regan" was arrested and is now in
jail here, charged with murder. A
daughter of the dead woman witnessed
the deed.

"We Want a Farm,
Not Buildings,"
Say Ranchers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 15.âMembers of
the Legislature who represent farming
communities insist that the State shall
do something at once to promote the
agricultural interests of California. Re
marks of this tenor are made:
"The proposition to spend $200,000 for
an agricultural building at Berkeley
and 50 cents for a farm is not accept
able. We want a farm of 800 or 900
acres, and it should not be in the im
mediate vicinity of Berkeley."
There will surely be an interesting
contest when the several bills bearing
on the proposed appropriation come be
fore the Legislature. Men who are fore
most in advocating what they term the
development of practical agricultureâ
the kind of development that will add
to the wealth of the State by inviting
young men to follow the pursuit of
farming for profit to themselves âcon-
tend that something more than theo
retical instruction is demanded. .The
splendid results achieved in Wisconsin,
lowa and Minnesota by object lessons
in the cultivation of the soil, the breed
ing of livestock and the management of
dairies are cited by the advocates of a
farm.
One of the chief advocates of an ap
propriation of $200,000 for a farm says:
"In the first place, we want a farm,
and later on an appropriation may be
made for a building. The professors at
Berkeley who are familiar with the
subject understand that a farm of
some magnitude is required. Our plan
is that the Governor, Lieutenant Gov
ernor, Chief Justice of / the Supreme
Court, the president of the university
and the president of the State Agricul
tural Society shall locate the farm.
When the location is made efforts to
enroll students will begin. The land will
be cultivated. For example, in corn
planting rows of several varieties will
be planted one month, a month later
and still a month later other rows will
be planted. Careful data of the growth,
the treatment and the production will
be made. In this manner the product
in lowa was increased 100,000,000 bush
els.
"Our* wheat, through lack of prac
tical knowledge on the part of our
farmers, is not producing, so It is ar
gued, the flour of the high standard at
tained years ago. The best flour of the
world should be produced here, as the
conditions of soil and climate are un
surpassed, but intelligent methods of
treatment and cultivation of the land
must be taught by practical demon
stration.
"The output of our dairy farms
should be increased. Practical lessons
in feeding should be given. Proper mix
ture of food would double the product
of butter without increasing the cost of
feeding.
"Above all, something must be done
to allure young men to the farm by
making the industry remunerative. Ap
plication of proper methods of cultiva
tion will engage the intellect .of the
farmer and banish the drudgery of or
dinary farm life."
COURT ASKED TO SETTLE
BOUNDARY LINE DISPUTE
Trinity and Mendocino Counties Each
Claim Ownership of Eighty Square
Miles of Territory.
REDDING. Jan. 15.âTrinity Coun
ty has brought suit against Mendocino
County in the Superior Court of Te
hama County for a settlement of the
boundary line dispute. Trinity com
plains that Mendocino is trying to rob
it of eighty square miles of land lying
on the Trinity side of the boundary
line that has been' recognized by both
counties for nineteen years. Trinity
County asks for Judgment recognizing
the boundury line and that Mendocino
be perpetually restrained from col
lecting taxes from residents north of
the line, and especially in the eighty
square miles of disputed territory.
LOS ANGELES AND CATALINA
CONNECTED BY WIRELESS
Installation of Plant Obviates Neces
sity of Relaying Messages at
San Pedro. .
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 15.âDirect
communication has been established
between Catalina Island and Los An
geles by the Pacific Wireless Tele
graph Company. Communication here
tofore with the island has been by
Western Union wire to San Pedro and
thence by wireless to Avalon, but with
the completion of the wireless station
at Los Angeles the necessity for using
the wire and the relaying of the mes
sages at San Pedro is obviated.

TREATY URGED
WITH GERMANY
Reciprocity Pact Desired
by the American Chamber
of Commerce in Berlin
LETTER TO ROOSEVELT
Believed That the Time Is
Now Ripe for Opening of
Negotiations on Subject
BERLIN, Jan. IB.âThe American
Chamber of Commerce of Berlin con
siders that the moment is favorable for
a movement, looking toward a reciproc
ity treaty between the United States
and GermJany, and especially so be
cause the new system of reciprocity
treaties which Germany has concluded
will probably affect American trade
adversely. The Chamber of Commerce
on December 23 sent a petition to Pres
ident Roosevelt, which in part follows:
The American Chamber of Commerce of Ber
lin, organized with the purpose of furthering
good relations between the United States and
Germany, especially In the direction of com
mercial affairs, and including among its mem
bers leading concerns of both nationalities
trading between the two countries, would pray
that you uree upon Congress and the other
authorities the presdag need of prompt action
for a recirjroclty treaty With Germany and
also of new laws and regulations for the ap
praisal of merchandise to replace the present
very faulty and often unjust system which has"
been and still is constantly causing much bit
terness of feeling among practically all the
importers of commodities into ports of the
United States.
The American Chamber of Commerce
offers its services in the collection of
information.
Many a woman who can converse in
five languages is unable to shut up in
one.

Weekly Gall $1.00 wMSOLDIER MURPHY RETURNS
FROM CANNIBAL LAND.
Says Wild Ifugao Tribes Treated Him Well
To have been the only white man who
has ever managed to exist without hav
ing his ribs picked clean among a tribe
of the fiercest cannibals of the island of
â¢Luzon, Philippines; to have been greet
ed as a friend by the savages' "big
chief," and to have succeeded in spend
. iv.g two years with as strange a people
as Uncle Sam has ever taken under the
protecting folds' of the Stars and Stripes
is the experience of L. M. Murphy, sol
â¢ eier of war and of fortune.
Mr. Murphy arrived from Manila on
â¢the transport Sherman yesterday after
noon, and it is an odd contradiction of
human fancy, as the civilized world un
derstands it, that he should be some
what sorry that he is come. Murphy
among cannibals is really happier than
Murphy in America. The traveler from
the jungle is a convert to "The Simple
Life," and he eschews only the spare
ribs of the human toastâthe victim of
a head hunt. Savagery is not so bad as
ft is painted, he says, and Murphy of
Bantoc has waxed fat and happy under
Its fanatical Influences.
"Northwestern and Northeastern Lu
â en," says Murphy, "is a no-man's
land, in which the mysteries of civiliza
tion have never even had opportunity
to present a problem." Abra, Ifugao
and La Ponta, deep in the frowning
ridges of a rugged mountain range,
teem with a human life that is as prim
itive as that which peopled the Sand
wich group when Cook found them.
The Spanish never dared to venture!
farther inland than Bantoc, seventy
miles from the country of the head
hunters, yet this one man, without arms
and with nothing but a small supply of
' provisions and a camera outfit, went
among the natives and was welcomed
by them.
"1 was a private in Com.pany C, Six
teenth said Murphy. "I got
my discharge aoput three years ago,
after serving three years in active ser
vice with my regiment. Being a pho
tographer of the amateur kind, I con
ceived the idea that it would be a good
business proposition if I could Succeed
â¢ in making a trip into the Ifugoa coun
. try and get the true story of that queer,
isolated people.
"When I started from Bantoc, the
nearest point to the rough Ifugoa
.lands. I was well provided with arms
and ammunition, but I had some diffi-,
culty with the constabulary, who seized
my pistols. The Spanish ex-officials at
Bantoc warned me not to make the
trip, as there had never been any ef
fort made by white men to enter the
domain of the cannibals. The province
is cut off from the main part of the
island, and as the people only bothered
the Filipinos living at theÂ«edge of their
territory the Spanish Government
thought it not worth while to send
troops into the mountainous district'to
"subjugate the tribe.
STARTS ON JOURNEY.
"I started on my journey, traveling
â¢from town to town with a few native
companions. Soon 1 found myself in a
wild region threaded by narrow forest
paths that led up and down steep
ridges. The tropic foliage entwined
about the thick growth of trees and
made it difficult to travel. After sev
eral weeks of hardship I reached the
home of the Ifugao and was told that
an old chief had a village near at hand,
but probably would not receive me
with much good grace. One afternoon
I wandered into the woods, and, hear
ing a noise in the brush, looked up and
saw a savage face glaring at me
through the jungle at a distance of not
â¢ more than ten yards.
"For several seconds we stood gazing
intently at each other. Then the old
thief broke the silence by repeating
'Amigo' (friend).
" 'Amigo,' " I replied.
" 'Marcano?'" he asked, mispro
nouncing 'Americano,' Spanish for
â¢ 'American.'
"I knew a few words of the Ifugao
dialect and tried to tell him that my
â¢ mission was a friendly one. He turned
' toward the forest and shouted some
â¢ thing and in a jiffy a band of savages
*vere gathered about me. I was given
â¢a sign to accompany them.
"I found the Ifugao tribe to be con
firmed head-hunters and cannibals.
â¢The .only distinction between a good
and a bad roast of human flesh was
â  whether it was that of a Filipino or a
â  member of a neighboring tribe. The
â¢Ifugao never eats one of his own race,
â  but he will travel a long distance, over
what seems to be an impassable coun
try to get a Filipino head. I never
witnessed cannibal feats, but was
shown ample evidence of them after
their termination.
"Physically the Ifugao and other
tribes of the provinces I visited are
splendid specimens of mankind, al
though they are of small stature.
Strange to say, In spite of their canni
balistic tendencies they are the great
est producers of vegetable supplies on
the island of Luzon. They are great
farmers and transport their green
stuff for many miles over the nar
row, treacherous roads of the seventy
miles between them and Bantoc. They
grow cabbages and radishes on their
AMERICAN SOLDIER WHO RETURNED ON THE TRANSPORT FHEPMAN
FROM THE PHILIPPINES TESTER DAT. AFTER A LONG SOJOURN AMONG
THE HEAD HUNTERS OF THE IFUGOA DISTRICT.
lands, and from rice make a very su- j
perior quality of bread. They have |
developed a peculiar sort of vegetable '
that looks like slippery elm and if a
small portion of it is placed in the
mouth it swells like rice in a kettle.
When boiled this substance tastes like
potato and is nearly as palatable.
"The men wear only a gee string
and occasionally a tapis cloth. The
women wear the tapis and a grass and
rattan covering that is a very beauti
ful piece of handiwork. The women do
most of the work in the rice fields.
TRIBES ARE IGNORANT.
"I was well treated during my stay
with the Ifugoa. Never at any time
had I occasion to complain and they"
even gave me privilege to select men or
women to carry my plates and camera
outfit during my excursions into the
country. Their huts are similar to those
of the Tagalo race except where there
is water and in this event the dwellings
are built over the streams or the inlets
on raised platforms. I was assigned to
comfortable quarters and treated with
deference. I learned to speak their
language and found that while there is
certainly more cruelty In thejr disposi
tions they have many traits Of charac
ter that are admirable, one of them
.being generosity.
"I left the tribe with regret and it is
my intention to return to them some
day, for I can with truth say that I
enjoyed my stay among them. There
were times, of course, when I had
disagreeable experiences passing from
one tribe to another and my life was
frequently in danger, but as soon as
DENIES STORY
OF MRS. DUKE
Murat Masterson, Attorney
of Colorado Springs, Says
Shooting Was Done by Her
COLORADO SPRINGS, Colo., Jan.
15.âMurat Masterson takes exception
to the statement attributed to Mrs.
Alice Webb, now Mrs. Brodie D. Duke,
to the effect that he shot her in the
hand in 1890. Masterson is a well
known attorney of this city. Said he
to-day:
I first met Mrs. Webb, then Mrs. Hopkin
son, in the summer of 1890 at the Washington
Park race course in Chicago. I was legal
counsel for Don Luis Huller, one of the largest
land and mine owners of Mexico. We had
several deals under way, and Mrs. Hopklnson
introduced me to several capitalists in New
York. I waited until May, 1891. and then
finally asked her for the papers which I had
turned over to her to present to her backers.
She was then living in a flat on Thirty-sev
enth street. At first she declined to give up
the business and papers, but after several
meetings she decided to do so.
When I entered her rooms I found two men
strangers to me, there. After a few words I
found that I was going to have trouble to se
cure possession of the papers, which I saw on
the mantel of the room. I was unarmed, but
finally drove her two friends from the room
and out through the hall. When I returned
to her room Mrs. Hopklnson held a pistol in
her hands and fired point blank at me, the
bullet whizring past my head and burying it
self In the door frame. I, renched the piitol
from her hand and threw it Into the hall. I
then took possession of the documents and left
the house. Both Mrs. Hopklnson and-myself
were arrested and taken to Jefferson Market
police station and next morning we were both
taken into the Police Court, but the cas* waa
dismissed and that was the end of it.

"The tribes of this out of the way
district know nothing of the changes
that have been wrought in the history
of the islands in the last five years.
They have seldom been disturbed and
care little whether Spaniards or Ameri
cans rule the archipelago. I took
nearly 2000 photographs of the tribes I
visited.
"The head-hunting expeditions were
conducted with a preliminary dance
and exchange of charms. The custom
is part of their religion and has been
handed down to them through genera
tions of cannibalism, of which they
have a fund of interesting legends. The
dance over, tbe hunting parties vanish
in the woods, thread their way through
hopeless tangles of jungle, and for days
press onward, subsisting on roots and
grasrhoppers. They return to the vil
lages in triumph with the heads and
bodies of their victims and in the orgies
that follow no civilized man would care
to take part."
Mr. Murphy thinks the United States
is going to have much trouble with the
Philippines unless the natives are ruled
with force. The Government policy has
been conciliatory, and the whites in
Manila and elsewhere are shown less
consideration than the Filipinos. This
policy is one of pacification, and to in
sure confidence in the American Gov
ernment, but Murphy says it is a
wrong one, as the Filipino knows a
master only when that master rules
him with iron hand.
MANY DESERT
NIEDRINGHAUS
Several Republicans Bolt
to Defeat Him for the
Senatorship of Missouri
KANSAS CITY. Nov. 15.âA special
to the Times from Jefferson City, Mo.,
says that enough Republicans have
bolted to defeat the ratification next
Tuesday of T. K. Niedringhaus, the
State chairman, recently named by the
Republicans in caucus to succeed
United States Senator Cockrell.
It was given out to-night that at a
dinner held Saturday night, attended
by nine members of the Legislature, an
agreement was reached to defeat
Niedringhaus. Major J. L. Blttinger
of St. Joseph, who presided at the con
ference, announced that besides the
members present five other Republi
cans had agreed to bolt the caucus
nominee if called upon to do so.
Representative Grace of St. Louis,
who introduced the resolution that
caused the investigation of the Nied
ringhaus fund held in St. Louis last
week, was present at the dinner.
A second conference of bolters was
held in Jefferson City to-night, and at
its conclusion some of those present
stated positively that Niedringhaus
would not be elected on Tuesday. The
followers of Niedringhaus, however,
are confident that he will be elected.
They maintain that the investigation
at St, Louis clearly vindicated him.
At the Republican caucus held here
on January 5 Niedringhaus was select
ed to succeed Senator Cockrell after a
spirited contest, in which five ballots
were taken. The final ballot stood:
Niedringhaus 58, Colonel R. C. Kerens
of St. Louis 29, Major William Warner
of Kansas City 1. L. F. Parker 4 and
Patrick Dyer of St. Louis 1.
The conference last Saturday night
was held following a dinner given by

ANNUAL BEPOBT MADE
Bailroad Work and Control
of Shipping Desired for
Islands by the Leaders
WASHINGTON. Jan. 15.âThe annual
report of the Philippine Commission to
the Secretary of War says that is soon
as the people came to understand that
the Government had both the ability
and the will to protect them they began
to co-operate cordially with the con
stabulary and other peace officers.
The non-Christian tribes of the pro
vince as soon as they understood the
Americans would protect them against
their ancient enemy, the Moros, were
only too willing to recognize American
i authority and receive the benefits of
| protection.
The report says that a pacific policy
j toward the Moros seemed to give them
| the impression that the Americans
j were afraid of them, but a short, deci
sive campaign gave them to under
; stand that the Americans had not only
I the purpose, but the power, to support
i authority and enforce order.
'"There is no reason," it is added, "to
i expect any further trouble with the
i Sulu Moros."
! The report, after referring to the sat
j isfactory adjustment of the friar lands
question, says there still continues to
â  be much controversy and ill feeling be-
I tween the independent Filipino church
I âor Aglipayans, as they are commonly
j called âand the Roman Catholic apos
! tolic church in regard to the churches,
convents and cemeteries in many locali
ties.
I The report urges that the commission
be given authority to make reductions
in the tariff schedules, the commission
saying in this connection that it always
I has felt there should exist the most
intimate trade relations between the
United States and the islands.
RECOMMENDATIONS MADE.
The commission makes the following
recommendations:
Reduction of the tariff on sugar and
| tobacco to not more than 25 per cent of
j the Dingley rate.
That authority be given the commis
! sion, with the approval of the Presl
i dent and Secretary of War, to issue
â¢ bonds from time to time for future im
| provements, not to exceed $75,000,000 in
' the aggregate.
Legislatiqn exempting all bonds is
! sued by the Philippine Government or
1 any of the provincial or municipal gov-
I ernments, not only from Federal or
â  Philippine taxation, but from State,
' county and municipal taxation in the
United States.
That control of the shipping be left
wolly to the discretion of the Philip
pine Commission, subject to the ap
! proval of the President and Secretary
jof War.
| That the application of the United
States coastwise navigation laws to the
trade between the Philippines and the
mainland of the United States be post
' pcned by Congressional action until
1 July 1, 1909, or, in the alternative, that
the coastwise laws of the United States
Ibe not made applicable to the trade
\ between the islands and the mainland,
except with a proviso or condition that
the rates upon imports from the Phil
ippines into the United States shall not
pay duty in excess of 25 per cent of the
Dingley tariff,
j Congressional action authorizing the
â¢ Philippine Commission, with the ap-
I proval of the President and Secretary
! of War, to encourage railroad eonstruc-
I tion in the islands by accompanying
1 the grants or franchises, when deemed
I necessary, with a guaranty by the
: Philippine Government of income on the
' amount of the investment, to be fixed
! in advance in the act of guaranty, the
amount of Income guaranteed not to
i exceed annually 4 per. cent of the fixed
principal.
SLAVERY AMONG MOROS.
In his report as Civil Governor of
! Moro province, General Wood says
slave trading hasj been brought prac
tically to an end: throughout the sea
coast Moro districts, although he says
it undoubtedly continues to some ex
tent between the inland Moros and the
â  savage tribes of the interior. General
I Wood says it would be difficult to
1 imagine a worse form of slavery, the
! slave having absolutely*no rights.
In submitting the report, Secretary
Taft says ladronism is confined to the
Lake Lanao region and the Island of
Jolo.
As to finances he says that from the
central government last year the
amount expended did not exceed
$1,200,000, as against $1,600,000 the pre
vious year. He adds that without
wasting money, $5,000,000 a year In the
maintenance of the public schools of
the Philippine islands could be readily
expended. The enrollment has Increas
ed from 183,000 to 263,000 In one year,
and there Is a demand for American
teachers and Filipino teachers in each
province. The total expenditures for
education last year, including those
from central provinces and municipal
governments, was nearly $2,000,000.
MISSION TO ABYSSINIA
BEARS FRUIT IN TRADE
Consul General Skinner Reports That
Business Between the Countries
Is on the Increase.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 15.âHow rap
idly the good results of the American
commercial expedition to Abyssinia,
which brought about the commercial
treaty between the United States and
that country, are'beginning to be felt
is seen from a report to the State De
partment from Skinner, the American
Consul General at Marseilles, in which
he says that many American orders
are now being shipped direct to Abys
sinia and that trade between the two
countries is increasing steadily.
Tower, the American Embassador
at Berlin, has reported that Germany,
following the example of this Govern
ment is sending a distinguished com
mission to King Menelik with a view
to increasing Germany's trade with
Abyssinia.

ST. LOUIS, Jan. 15.âLast night
Colonel R. C. Kerens gave a dinner to

hie home in St. Louis, and as a result

WILL SOON RESUME
BATTLE OF SHAKHE
Mistchenkos Raid Believed to Be the
â  Forerunner of an Advance
by Kuropatkin.
ST. PETERSBURG, Jan. IB.âThe
War Office does not admit that the ap
pearance of General Mlstchenko's cav
alry southwest of Liaoyang is more
than a daring expedition designed to
strike the railroad at several points
and interrupt the transportation of
General Nogi's guns from Port Arthur
to Manchuria, but from certain indi
cations it seems possible that it is pre
liminary to operations of magnitude.
General Mistchenko has more than
20,000 horsemen, and, moving rapidly,
might be able to seriously damage
ccrnmunication in the rear of Liayong.
Dispatches from some Russian cor
respondents vaguely hint at very im
portant developments and the military
writer for the Novoe Vremya expresses
the opinion that the long calm since
the battle of Shakhe is about to be
broken. It is also noteworthy that
there have been no official dispatches
from General Kuropatkin for two
days.
LONDON. Jan. 16. âA dispatch to
Lloyds' from Yinkow, dated January
li">, gives a report that General Mits
chenko's raiding force was cut off on
its way back by 8000 Japanese dis
patched from Sanlihoo by General
Oku. This report, however, is not con
firmed from any other source.
The Daily Telegraph's Chefu corre
spondent says the raid was only a
divergence from a mass of cavalry now
moving down the banks of the Liao
River and the exceptional mildness of
the season affords a chance for the
repetition of such operations.
The tone of the rescript addressed
by Emperor Nicholas to the army and
navy is regarded here as putting an
end to all present hopes of the possi
bility of mediation or peace and as
indicating the likelihood that General
KuropUtkin soon will resume the of
fensive.
HUANCHANG, Friday, Jan. 13.â
There has been no serious fighting for
several days, though night skirmishes
on both sides continue. The most im
portant action recently was that in
which Caotains Koslnoff and Troitgki,

and Tkhangay and captured a large
amount of forage at both places. Gen
eral Linevitch has been inspecting the

and supplies are in excellent condi
tion. The Japanese on their side are
pushing sapping operations toward the
Russian lines at several points, but
have not made serious progress.

PROSPERS DESPITE WAR.
Increased Exports Denote Growth in
Japan's Commerce.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 15. â
Department has received an interest
ing report from Griscom, the Ameri
can Minister to Tokio, dealing with
the present economic conditions in
Japan and her trade relations with the
United States. Griscom. in several sta
tistical contributions compiled from
official sources, compared the first
nine months of the present conflict
with the same time last year.
The present year shows an increase
in exports of $6,424,159 and an in
crease Â»n imports of $10,080,597. Dur
ing the same period the of
exports in specie and bullion amount
ed to $41,403,394 and the increase in
imports to $1,842,370.
During the period in review exports
to the United States were valued at
$33,898,622 and imports therefrom at
$19,719,486. showing, as compared
with the same period of 1903, an in
crease in exports of $3,919,277 and an
increase in imports of $2,702,538. Raw
silk was the principal item of export,
showing a marked increase. There
was a reduced export of tea and coal.
Kerosene oil, leather, rails, flour and
sugar were imported from the United
States in larger quantities than dur
ing the same period last year, while
raw cotton imports declined.
Griscom describes the readiness with
which the Japanese domestic loans
have been floated.
The total amount of the national
debt of Japan at the end of Septem
ber stoodÂ»at about $444,216,000, mak
ing a debt per capita of about $9 46.
BOER METHODS FOLLOWED.
Port Arthur Trenches Similar to Those
Used in South Africa.
TOKIO, Jan. 15.âA naval officer who
has returned from Port Arthur, dis
cussing conditions ther/s to-day, said:
"The condition of the Russian war
vessels and the town is much better
than hoped for. Evidently there was
much fortifying of the place after the
siege commenced. Two Hundred and
Three Meter Hill had no permanent
works. The trenches were similar to
those used by the Boers in their late
war with Great Britain. It seems that
the Russians had no general electric
scheme, movable engines, dynamos or
powerful fort searchlights.
"The Russian naval officers yet say
little and It is impossible to ascertain
whether our fire or the opening of the
seacocks by the Russians themselves
sank their ships in the harbor.
"The hospitals were scattered and
were fairly well kept. They are still
being used for wounded prisoners.
"It is understood that up to the capit
ulation pf the fortress the garrison re
ceived only special allowances of
money, but before the surrender Gen
eral Stoessel paid them off. This, to
gether with the constant purchase of
supplies by the Russians from Junk
men, may explain-why no treasure fell
into the hands of the Japanese.
"The treatment of the Japanese pris
oners at Port Arthur did not compare
with that accorded Russian prisoners
here. At Port Arthur the Japanese
prisoners were given poor food and
were not given fuel. Only twice were
they permitted outside their prison."
It is reported that hundreds of Jap
anese fishermen have been sent to
Port Arthur to assist in the clearing
out of mines.
STOESSEL'S MEN UNRULY.
British Correspondent Says Drunken
ness Was Common in Stronghold.
LONDON, Jan. 15.âUnder date of
January 15, the Port Arthur
spondent of the Times says:
"General Nogl, attended by his staff,
the divisional commanders and foreign
attaches, reviewed detachments of all
arms in the new Town Square to-day.
There was a stirring display. The gen
erals afterward marched through the
town, which shows few signs of the
bombardment. No casualties occurred
among the women or children who lived
in the houses throughout the siege.
Champagne was always obtainable.
There waa little dystentery or typhus,
but much scurvy, owing to the lack of
vegetables.
"It seems that General Stoessel was
responsible for the fortifying of Roju
Hill. Many of his military officers were
useless, applying for leave on occasions
of attack and leaving their commands
to sergeants. The naval officers were
useless and generally ! drunk. The
whole navy was demoralized by the
death of Admiral Makaroff. which pro
duced marked apathy. At a confer
ence the capitulation, some
of the fort commanders voted for fur-
ther resistence, but they were over
ruled by General Stoessel. When news
of the capitulation became public, sol
diers looted the stores of 5000 bottles of
vodka, resulting in terrible orgies in
the streets. Troops sent to quell the
disturbance joined in with the revelers.
"The food supply was sufficient to
last three months, but there was no
meat except horse flesh. No private
stores were seized. The steamship
King Arthur brought 5000 sacks of
flour early in December."
GRIEVANCE AGAINST ENGLAND
Russian Press Claims Japanese Are
Using British Island as Rase.
ST. PETERSBURG, Jan. 15.âSome
of the newspapers here are claiming
that the Japanese are using the lower
island of Laulian, near Borneo, as a
base, and that the British are conceal
ing fact by preventing communi
cation between Borneo and Singapore,
on the ground that the cable is under
going repair.
The report from Washington that it
is believed there that the powers will
informally protest to Russia against
the notice served by her that she will
consider herself no longer bound to
regard the neutrality of China if Ja
pan continues to violate it, has created
astonishment here, where it is held
that any protest should be directed
against the action of Japan, against
which Russia has now thrice warned
the powers. Russia's position is that
she is bound under the circumstances
to take steps for the protection of her
own interests.
Stoessel Thanks the Kaiser.
NAGASAKI. Jan. 16.âThe British
steamship Roseley. which was cap
tured on January 11 by the
Japanese cruiser Tekiwa in the
Sea of Japan, has arrived at Sasebo,
where she will be tried by the prize
court on the charge of having at
tempted to carry coal to Vladivostok.
Generall Stoessel has cabled his
thanks to the Emperor of Germany
for the decoration conferred upon
him. Passage for General Stoessel
and his party has been booked to
Port Said.
PLAN TO HARMONIZE
LIVESTOCK INTERESTS
Efforts Being Made to Unite Seceding
Faction With Parent
Organization.
DENVER, Jan. 15.âNegotiations
have been*opened by the officers of the
National Livestock Association with
the American Cattle Growers' Associa
tion, which was organized in this city
last Friday by cattlemen, who seceded
from the stockmen's convention, with
a view to establishing harmonious re
lations between the two organizations.
It is proposed to have the new asso
ciation of cattle growers affiliate with
the parent organization exactly as
does the National Wool Growers' As
sociation.
President Frank J. Hagenbarth and
several members of the executive com
mittee of the National Livestock As
sociation have applied for membership
in the Cattle Growers' Association, be
lieving that it can be made of benefit
to the united livestock Interests of the
country.
FRIENDS FEAR AGED MAN
HAS MET WITH FOUL PLAY
Disappearance of Well-Known Butte
County Veterinarian Is the Cause
of Much Concern.
BIGGS, Jan. 16.âThe friends .of
Charles S. Brown, a pioneer veterinary
surgeon fbf this place, are greatly
alarmed owing* to the absence of
Brown from his home and the fact
that no one has seen or heard from
him since he left John Richer's livery
stable in Oroville Saturday, January 7,
for his home in Biggs. Brown's dis
appearance has caused his many
friends to fear that he has met with
a serious accident or become a victim
of foul play.
Brown is a pensioner of the Civil
War, aged 66 years. He Is well known
and respected throughout Butte and
adjoining counties.
SKIPPER'S WIFE MUTINIES
ON SCHOONER ANNIE SMALL
In a Discussion With the Mate Mrs.
Wlrschuleit Poors Boiling
Coffee Over-Him.
If the schooner Annie Small had
been on the high seas instead of the*
placid mud flats near the Fourth
street bridge Mrs. Wlrschuleit, wife of
the skipper, would be In the brig for
mutiny. During an argument on board
the vessel she smote the first mate,
Alfred Howard, on the nose and
poured a half gallon of hot coffee over
his head.
Mrs. Wlrschuleit is one of the crew,
according to Howard, but she seems
to imagine that she is Lord High Ad
miral of the bay scow fleet During
the absence of the captain the mate
piped his crew, consisting of Mrs.
Wlrschuleit, on deck to dip the en
sign to a passing vessel.
"Man the foretop gallants and dip
the ensign," shouted the mate.
"Dip it yourself," retorted the crew,
as it lifted its skirts and strode
haughtily into the galley.
"Mutiny!" cried the mate, and fol
lowed her to remonstrate. The lady
swung a wicked right to his eye. He
countered on the gentle mariner's jaw,
sending her to the ropes. The lady
sidestepped and seized a pot filled
with boiling coffee and drenched the
mate with the contents.
The fair crew was towed to the
Southern police station and Howard
put in at the Central Emergency Hos
pital for. repairs. He will be on the
dry-dock for several days. He says
that next time his gentle mariner
starts a mutiny the trouble will be
settled with a belaying pin.
ARIZONA MUST
BE ELIMINATED
Small Hope for Statehood
Bill Unless Compromise
Is Quickly Agreed Upon
BABD'S PLAN FAVOBED
Would Strike From the Meas
ure the Names of Both
Southwestern Territories
WASHINGTON, Jan. 15.âThe Inten
tion Is to keep the Statehood bill to the
front In the Senate during the present
week, with the hope on the part of its
friends that they may be able to get
a vote upon it. Thus far. the oppo
nents of the measure have held out
stubbornly against naming a day for
a vote, and while they do not say in
explicit terms that it is their intention
to filibuster against the passage of the
bill, it is evident that unless a compro
mise can be reached for the elimina
tion of Arizona from the measure they
will make an effort to prevent a vote
before the close of the present session.
On this account there is coming to
be more or less discussion of plans for
tbe modification of the bill and most
of the talk gravitates toward the sug
gestion of Senator Foraker for separa
tion of the votes of Arizona and New
Mexico on the question of admission,
or that suggested by Senator Bard,
confining the operation of the bill to
the creation of the State of Oklahoma
out of the Territory of Oklahoma and
Indian Territory and entirely eliminat
ing Arizona and New Mexico from the
bill. A number of Republicans have
announced themselves favorable to
either of these suggestions
The important work before the
House this week is the disposition of
the Swayne case.
BURNING SAWDUST NEARLY
SUFFOCATES THE FIREMEN
Troublesome Blaze in the Schwarz
child & Sulzberger Packing
Plant at Chicago.
CHICAGO, Jan. 15.âThirty-flve
companies of firemen to-night fought
a Are in the beef storage department
of the Schwarzchild & Sulzberger
Company and when the Are was finally
put out more than $5000 damages had
been done. The Are caught in saw
dust that had been packed into a
space eight feet wide between the
wall 3. The streams of water poured
upon it had no noticeable effect for
four hours.
The volume of smoke was so dense
that the Aremen were compelled to
relieve each other every few minutes to
escape suffocation.

Formus stole a $3CO diamond rins in

WANTS TO KILL
SALOON EVIL
General Mac Arthur Is Deter
. mined to End Sale of
Liquors at Post Gates
ASKS PUBLIC FOR HELP
Â»
Thinks State Should Pass a
Bill So Strong That Au
thorities Must Enforce It
General Mac Arthur, commander of
the division of the Pacific, is making
a great struggle to remedy the saloon
evil that exists about the entrances to
the Presidio. Through the newspa
â¢ pers, "he appeals to the public of Ban
Francisco to help him in his efforts.
General Mac Arthur greatly regrets
â¢that the Keane bill introduced at the
session of the Senate on January 2 tor
prohibiting the selling or giving away
intoxicating liquors within 1000 feet
of any entrance to a United States
military reservation should have come
â¢to such an untimely end. He says he
understands that one of the members,
when this bill was introduced jumped
up and said that if it was passed, pro
hibiting saloons within 1000 feet of
the' entrance to the Presidio, it would
result in closing up a lot of hotels.
Upon this statement Keane cut down
the distance from 1000 feet to 500
feet.. The bill was then tabled.
General Mac Arthur says he wants
to be the father of a bill, and is will
ing to be quoted as saying that he
wants a bill passed so strong that the
municipal authorities must enforce it.
He declares that a bill to carry out
this object and assist the military au
thorities should be so constructed as
to prevent the establishment of any
saloon, or liquor selling resort, within
1000 feet of the reservation lines. This
bill should apply to all posts within
the State that are ndw established or
. may be established.
Some good has already come from
the exertions of General Mac Arthur,
through his persistence in insisting
that Mayor Schmltz should see that
the law was enforced regarding the
saloons at the Presidio gates. As a
result of the recent efforts of the mu
nicipal authorities, a number of ar
rests of saloon-keepers have been
made. Some of those arrested have
gone free owing to technicalities, but
three have been convicted by Juries,
after having demanded such trials.
Judge Cabanlss, in passing sentence,
has indicated his intention to punish
the violators of the law with increas
ing severity, and he has already im
posed 1300 fines in these cases.
Unless something is done, in this
matter General Mac Arthur says the
Presidio will in a measurejreceive the
same censure that was visited upon
Bremerton. It will be remembered
that the riotous proceedings at that
place, caused by the close proximity
of the saloons to the navy yard there,
caused the Government to close the
shops. The Government even went so
far as to declare that unless the evil
there was suppressed, the navy yard
would be abandoned altogether.
Thereupon the citizens of Bremerton
got together and forced the common
council to pass an ordinance to pre
vent the sale of liquor there, so the
navy yard Is still open. Of ceurse the
Government has no intention of mov
ing the present post from the Presidio,
hut General Mac Arthur says .if the
citizens of California do not care to
assist him and the military authorities
In closing up the grog shops and
brothels within a reasonable distance
of the reservations it is not likely the
Government will be disposed to make
further allotments for the beautifying
and adornment of the military posts
within this State.

Tries Suicide With Tonic.
OAKLAND, Jan. 16.âDespondent
because'his wife had left him after his
prolonged drinking bout, Thomas
Deasy, a gardener at the Hall of Rec
ords, drank a quantity of strychnine
tonic this morning in an attempt to
commit suicide at his room, northeast
corner of Broadway and Eleventh
street. T. V. Crabtree and B. J. Gllar
din. who witnessed the act. called the
police. Deasy was revived at the Re
ceiving Hospital. He will recover.
his share in a relative's estate. Imme
diately he quit work, deciding he could
make a fortune by "playing the
races." He went to Ascot Park and
tried until the last cent was gone. He
had volubly admired Mrs. Parker's
valuable gem and told her he intended
to buy it. Thus he learned where she
kept the 'circlet.
One day after he had been examining
the diamond the owner missed it. In
quiry revealed that Formus had also
disappeared. The Los Angeles police
traced the ring to a pawnshop, where
it was recovered. Formus, with the
money he had raised on the gem. came
to Oakland. At the Emeryville track
he lost all but $4 of the fruits of his
Jheft. He was arrested on a telegraph
ed warrant from the south.
Detective J. W. Murphy of Los An
geles departed on the Owl train to
night with Formus.
HIGH BRED BIRDS WILL
BE EXHIBITED IN OAKLAND
First Exclusive Pigeon Show Ever
Held on Pacific Coast to
Open Next Week.
OAKLAND. Jan. 15. âThe first ex
clusive show of runt pigeons and other
varieties ever held on the Pacific
Coast will be given in Oakland next
week, under the auspices of the Amer
ican Runt Pigeon Club. The show
will open Wednesday, January 18, and
will continue until Saturday evening,
January 21. Six hundred entries have
already been received by the secre
tary of the show committee, many of
the birds entered being prize winners
at the World's Fair in St. Louis.
A number of entries have been re
ceived from fanciers in the East, and
nearly every loft on the Pacific Coast
will be represented. The exhibition
will be held in Foresters' Hall at
Thirteenth and Clay streets, and the
show will be open ,from 9 a. m. until
midnight each day.
The members of the executive and
show committee are: E. L. Andrews,
president; R. V. Moore, secretary; Dr.
W. J. Smythe, C. B. Jennings, J. H.
Crow, A. B. Woods, Percy Weeks, R.
E. Nordyke and Martin Nielsen.
The birds will be judged by J. A.
Macdonald, E. L. Andrews and R. W.
Moore. One hundred special prizes
have been offered, and three silver
cups will be nresented as grand prizes.

Care for Last Rites Over
Mother.
OAKLAND, Jan. 15. â Separate
funerals were held to-day over the

land's sons took charge of his funeral,
while Mrs. Rowland's daughters at
tended to the last rites over her re
mains. '
Services for the husband were held
this afternoon at McMands', under
taking parlors at Seventh and Castro
streets. The Rev. I. N. Halliday, as

Pacific Company employes, former fel
low workmen with Rowland. Inter

The funeral of Mrs. Rowland was
held to-day at Byron, the home of her
parents. The body was sent there yes
terday in charge of the daughters,
Mrs. Ethel Day, Mrs. F. W. Sonder
leiter and Mrs. Antonio Silva.
