THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 16, 1905.
COMES TO AID
OF WHEELER
Santa Barbara Chamber of
Commerce Indorses Plan
for Agricultural Work
URGES APPROPRIATIONS
Southern Californians Desire
to See University Depart
ment Greatly Broadened
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 15.
President Benjamin Ide Wheeler to
day received a communication from the
Chamber of Commerce of Santa Bar
bara which embodies encouraging re
sults of his visit to the State Farmers'
Institute recently held there. The
Chamber of Commerce has adopted
resolutions relating to the broadening
and general extension of the univer
sity's work in the agricultural depart
ment, and these have been sent to the
university authorities. They are as fol
lows:
Whereas, During recent years the agricul
tural college of the University of California
has teen rendering lnvealuable service to the
farming interests of the State; and '
Whereas, the additions of a department *6f
dairying, a department of plant pathology, a
bureau of correspondence inetruction. a depart
ment of irrigation, and the extension of the
work in viticulture, entomology, bacteriology,
veterinary science and institute instruction are
deeply appreciated and call for encouragement
to further endeavors; and
Whereas, The expansion along all these
lines has made the present buildings of the
college utterly insufficient for its needs; and
Whereas, We believe there should be large
and generous treatment of the college on the
part of the State, that it should be fully and
properly equipped, and that it should be the
peer of the agricultural college of any State
in the Union;
Therefore, Be it resolved, by the Board of
Directors of the Chamber of Commerce of
Santa Barbara County, That we recommend
that the Legislature provide the means for a
building at Berkeley worthy of the Depart
ment of Agriculture and equal to that pro
posed for the Department of Mining; that ap
propriation be made for a proper farm for illus
tration and instruction, and that increased
provision be made for special InvestigaUon of
plant disease* and the study of special prob
lems affecting the plant and animal products
of the State.
Resolved, That copies of these resolutions bo
sent to our representatives in the Legislature
and to his Excellency, Governor Pardee.
Announcement was made to-day at
Ihe university offices that one of the
important features of the summer
school curriculum will be contributed
by Mrs. Anna B6tsford Comstock of
Cornell University. She is known for
hfcr work in nature study and agricul
tural courses.
Mrs. Comstock is to give at the sum
mer school here two independent
courses, one to be the regular naturer
study course, to consist each week of
four lectures and a conference; the
second, a course on nature-study liter
ature. In this course she will discuss
first the nature-study manuals; second,
the popular books on nature which
may aid the teacher in nature-study,
and third, nature-study literature. This
last will include lectures on the writ
ings of Gilbert White, Richard Jeff
ries, Isaac Walton, Thoreau and others,
and also discussions of nature poetry.
Mrs. Comstock is well known as a
natural history artist and wood en
graver and received a bronze medal at
the Pan-American Exposition in 1901
for her wood engraving. In 1899-1900
she was a lecturer at Stanford Univer
sity.
She is the author of several books,
one of which is "Problems of the Six-
Footed," and is Joint editor with her
husband, Professor John Henry Corn
stock, at one time United States En
tomologist at Washington, and at pres
ent professor of entomology at Cornell
University, of several Important works
in entomology.
Talking Tree of Kentucky.
Out on the farm of Will Albert, near
Heath, this county, the people of that
section are yet wrought up over the
"talking tree" that has been there for
some time. Enormous crowds con.inue
to congregate there almost every Sun
day to hear the strange noises that
emanate from the tree. The voice can
be distinctly heard and says: "There
are treasures buried at my roots." A
party consisting of the most reliable
citizens of the country visited the tree
not long since to make a thorough in
vestigation for themselves as to the
noises being heard. They listened pa
tiently for several hours and were pre
paring to leave for home when a sud
den crash, which has been given many
times before the marvelous production
of a human voice, came. The mystery
yet remains unsolved, and so great has
the number of people been who have
gone there in the past several months
that the tree is now dead, caused by
the continuous tramping on the earth
surrounding the tree. The only theory
that has been suggested is that a man
was killed under the tree in 1862, and
while many do not believe in "spirits,"
the facts are so plain and the voice can
be so distinctly heard that they can
not dispute the fact. A family of peo
ple who lived there many years ago
became so frighened from the voice
that they sold their farm tat a sacri
fice and went West and are now liv
ing In Texas.âPaducah News.
Senator Beat the Chaplain.
Chaplain Edward Everett Hale has
a lively interest in the spiritual wel
fare of his Senatorial charges. ''Sen
ator," said he to a well-known mem
ber of that body recently, "I have
never heard you say you were a mem
ber of the church." "Oh, yes!" replied
this worldly man of the upper house
in polite phrases. "I was a member of
the old Plymouth Church," naming
also the city where he resided. "So
glad to hear it," rejoined Dr. Hale in
those hearty tones for which he is
famous. Two or three days later he
encountered the same Senator and
warily observed: "I have been looking
up the* history of old Plymouth
Church, but I find it w.as burned
eighteen years ago." "Very true," said
the Senator, unperturbed: "but I be
longed there twenty years ago and
have always retained my member
ship."âChicago Chronicle. '
Growth of the Churches.
Despite the clatter about outworn
creeds and the anxious look in the faces
of amateur diagnosticians as they sit
up with the church and feel its pulse,
the statistics are still on the tide of the
church. They show It vital, not mori
bund. The figures for practically every
denomination show a percentage of
growth during 1904 larger than the in
crease In population. There is not such
a drift away from traditional theology
as many would have the world believe.
The average person Is not as deter
mined to break away from the faith of
the fathers as Is currently represented.
The truth Is the pulpit has always
taken far more interest in theological
puzzles than the pews, and there can
be a mighty churning of the doctrinal
waters without the church rank and
file becoming disturbed.âNew Tork
Globe.
RECORDS SHOW
BIG INCREASE
More Than Two Thousand
New buildings Erected in
Alameda County in Year
REALTY MARKET ACTIVE
Dealers Report Better Busi
ness Than Ever Before
During Month of January
Oakland Office, San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 15.
The records of new buildings in Ala
meda County, kept by the secretary of
the Alameda County Planing Mill
Owners' Association, which are more
nearly complete than the records kept
by municipal authorities, show that ap
proximately 2200 residence structures
were erected in Oakland, Alameda and
Berkeley during 1904. This far exceeds
the number in any previous year and
indicates better than anything else the
rapid development of the eastern shore
of the bay.
Official records kept by Sanitary In
spector S. F. Gemmell, and published
by the Oakland Health Department,
show that 853 new buildings were erect
ed in Oakland alone.
From tni .se statistics a conservative
estimate would place the year's actual
increase in the population of Alameda
County at 10,000, and that of Oakland
at about 50u0. Many of the newcom
ers to Oakland business men,
who have not only erected homes, but
have entered at once into the com
mercial life of the community.
The approximate cost of the new
buildings in Oakland, Alameda and
Berkeley, and of the improvements in
stores and offices, figured from the es
timates of a number N of well-known
architects and builders, was about $6,
--000,000. The cost of new buildings in
the remaining sections of the county is
estimated by the same men, among
whom are Councilman W. J. Baccus
and J. B. McKeon, secretary of the
Planing Mill Owners' Association, to
be about $2,000,000, making the total
amount expended in new buildings in
the county approximately $8,000,000.
Mr. McKeon, in discussing the
growth of the county, as well as of
Oakland, said to-day:
After reviewing the records of thle office
and estimation the number of Jobs supplied
with mill work by the mills ownod by mem
bers of the association, I believe that the closest
possible approximate estimate of the residence
buildings erected in Oakland, Berkeley and
Alameda'during the year of 1904 would place
the number at about 2200.' About 70 per cent
of these consist of two-story or higher struc
tures, the remaining 30 per cent being one
story houses and cottages.
The reason of any uncertainty as to building
figures Â« that in the past any contractor de
siring to erect any class of structure for
almost any purpose could do so, provided that
the fire ordinance and street regulations were
not involved, without applying to the authori
ties for a permit of any kind. Consequently
no .complete record of this most important
factor in the development of our city has been
kept and it is therefore impossible to secure
accurate statistics as to the increase in the
building industry during the year Just ended.
Until very recently no city ordinance has
required persons erecting buildings within the
city limits of Oakland to apply (or a permit
from the Board of Public Works. I understand
that the new building ordinance, which has
Just been passed to print, requires permits
for all building work.
Sanitary Inspector Stewart F. Gem
mell, said:
The reports of the Sanitary Inspector's office
for the year of, 1904 show that 853 new build
ings were erected in the city of Oakland dur
ing the twelve months Just ended. These re
ports deal only with the buildings in which
new plumbing has been done, taking no
account of the many thousands of dollars ex
pended in improvements in stores, office build
ings an dwellings, of which no record has been
kept by the Sanitary Inspector. Following is
the detailed report of new buildings erected
within the city limits during the last year,
as submitted to the Board of Health: Private
dwellings, 588; flats, 217; additions, 69; stores
and dwellings, 32; new work in old buildings,
84; warehouses, 3; hospitals, 2; banks, 2; fac
tories, 2; one business college, one theater,
one cannery, one engine house, one railroad
station, one freight office and one nurses'
home.
The present condition of the local
real estate market that the
record of the present year will be even
better than that of 1904. At this sea
son immediately after the opening of
the new year, dealers expect a dull
market, but this year they have been
agreeably disappointed. The market
is much more active than ever before
during January and there is every
reason to believe the present prosper
ity will continue.
The erection of the new plant for the
Seattle Brewing Company, to be lo
cated at First and Jackson streets,
has already begun. The contract calls
for its completion within ninety days.
The new building will be 62x120 feet,
and two stories in height, of brick and
concrete, and will cost $20,000. It Will
have the most modern machinery.

the Oakland Real Estate Association of
the Hotel Metropole the annual elec

ry X* Holcomb; executive committeeâ

der's report showed a good balance on

A committee of three members was
appointed to interview the Board of
Health, looking toward the removal
from the outside of its building of the
large map showing the location of con
tagious disease, as strangers are apt to
get the impression that Oakland is a
very unhealthy place unless they look
very closely, as few do, and see how
few cases there really are.
Articles of incorporation have just
been filed with the County Clerk by
the Overland Planing Mill Company,
which intends at once to begin the
erection of a planing mill at Emery

KINIRY GIVES
SIGN OF FEAR
Comment on Detective's Nar
rative Shows Burglar Is
Thinking of Possible Fate
BREAKS HIS RESERVE
Says He Might as Well Have
Been Killed in the Fight
That Led ~to His Capture
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 15.
David Kiniry, awaiting trial on a
charge of murdering Policeman James
,H. Smith, to-day for the flrfet time since
his return to Oakland in custody gave
expression to a realization of his peril
ous position. \t was during a conversa
tion this afternoon at the County Jail
with Detective J. W. Murphy of Los
Angeles that the hitherto phlegmatic
burglar made a suggestion that indi
cated he had been thinking of his pos
sible doom.
Murphy told Kiniry that the night the
burglar had his battle at the Doheny
residence in Los Angeles he (Murphy)
could have killed the fleeing house
breaker if the detective had known in
time whether Kiniry was the fugitive
or the pursuing watchman. Murphy
was attracted to the grounds by the
pistol shots. He entered the place with
his revolver drawn just as Kiniry
brushed by him, within a few feet of
the officer.
"I didn't dare to shoot then because
I did not know what was doing," ex
plained Murphy. "If I had fired at you
I should surely have killed you, for I
was near enough to have put a bullet
through your head."
"It might have been as well for me if
you had known and had shot me dead,"
replied Kiniry, to the surprise of his
hearers. Never before had the prisoner
so much as Intimated he had given any
thought to the ordeal of a murder trial
he must shortly face.
Kiniry has been advised by his moth
er and stepfather that they purpose
providing funds for his defense. They
have written to him several times, sug
gesting names of prominent Oakland
attorneys he might consult. According
to his Jailers Kiniry has exhibited no
special concern as to that subject. If
anything, he has been dilatory in re
plying to the offers of financial help
from his people.

DEMANDS ROOM
FOR CHILDREN
School Superintendent Says
Conditions in Berkeley
Call for New Buildings
WATERMAN'S REMARKS
Urgent Need He Says of Pro
viding Additional Facili
ties for the Schools
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
214fLCenter Street, Jan. 15.
Supplementing the call of the Board
of Education for a bond election, when
citizens will pass upon the plan to is
sue bonds for $150,000, to be used in
building new schools has come the fol
lowing statement from Superintendent
S. D. Waterman, referring to conditions
as they appear now, since the opening
of the new term of school:
The second term of the current school year
has opened very successfully. The'two un
graded classes have been qrganized, one in the
Lincoln School building, the other in the Mc-
Klnley. An additional class of sixth and sev
enth grade pupils has been opened in the Alls
ton Way building. The pupils for this class
are from fhe North Berkeley district.
The need of six more rooms at the Whlttler
school was never more plain than now. The
four classes at the Allston Way building ars s
composed of pupils living, for the greater part,
north of University avenue. The two classes
at the Rose street school ought to be trans
ferred to the Whittier, making sixteen classes.
These pupils who have been attending school
at buildings at a distance from their homes
have accepted the situation gracefully and
the patrons of the schools have, as a rule,
been very Datient.
The twelve rooms built within the last
eighteen months are filled, several additional
classes have been formed and still in many
of the buildings the classes are crowded.
This Is notable in the upper grades in the
Lincoln, the McKinley, the Whittier and the
La Conte schools. The only relief that will
be permanent must come In additional rooms
and buildings.

IS DEPORTED
Young Government Clerk in
. Manila Sent Home Under
â¢ Guard of an Army Nurse
Pathetically protesting against the
judgment of a military board of sur
geons thai declared her insane and
caused her deportation from the city
of Manila, Miss Lowry, a well-known
' young woman of Cleveland, Ohio, is re
strained of her liberty at the Occiden
tal Hotel, awaiting the action to-day
of the local army officials that will
fret, htr from the custody of an army
nurse and place her in the hands of
Miss Lowry was a passenger on the
transport Sherman, which arrived yes
rdaj from the Philippines. She was
;i â¢ ompanied by Miss B. M. Gertich, an
army nurse, who had been detailed in
Manila to bring the unfortunate young
woman to San Francisco. Here the
la Iter's aunts. Miss C. L. Clark of
Cleveland and Miss G. Woodruff of St.
Davids, Ohio, haye been impatiently
awaiting the arrival of their relative
for several days, it is the second time
that the young woman has been sent
away from the Philippines and both
times it w as for the same reasonâmen-
tal irresponsibility. Four years ago
Mi s Lowry was appointed from Cleve
lt>nd to a civil service position in Man
ila and immediately departed for the
islands in company with several other
young women, who also had been as
signed to duties there.
For a time all went well and her
relatives in Cleveland received pleas
ing letter? discriblng her interesting ex
p- riences in her new home. One day they
were suddenly startled by the informa
tion that Miss Lowry had got into
trouble in Manila and was about t<> be
deported. Later news was to the effect
that the young woman had attempted
to kill Albert Easthagen. a fellow clerk,
In whose company she often had been
seen.
ATTACKS FELLOW CLERK.
According to the stories that reached
this Fide of the Pacific, Mm Lowry
one day approached Easthagen and
suddenly drawing a revolver, pulled the
trigger. It snapped, however, without
discharging the cartridge and a second
later Miss Lowry was disarmed. She
was placed under arrest and for sev
eral months kept under the observation
of army surgeons, who finally recom
mended that their patient he sent
home. Accordingly Miss Lowry was
placed aboard a transport and made
the homeward journey by way of the
. Buefc canal and New York, the hope of
her army friends being that a long
ocean voyage might cure her mind.
About four months ago Miss Lowry
disappeared from Cleveland and quiet
ly made her way back to Manila, w here
sh< was later found acting in a strange
manner, apparently laboring under
hallucinations, one of which, It was
afterward discovered, was that Fili
pinos had been engaged to poison her.
Major H. Carter called the attention
of the Manila Board of Health to the
young woman's sad predicament and
that body ordered her again deported.
By arrangements military au
thorities Miss Gertich, an army nurse,
was detailed to accompany Miss Lowry
to San Francisco on the transport
Sherman, and the relatives of the lat
ter were advised to meet the young
woman on her arrival here.

from Manila Miss Clark . nd Miss
Woodruff prepared for a Journey to this
city, and arriving here several days
ago they went to the Russ House to
await the coming of the Sherman. They
were at the dock yesterday to meet
Miss Lowry, but were not permitted
immediately to take her in charge, the
Government's instructions being that
Miss Gertich should be :elieved of her
responsibilities only on the authority
of the local army officials. Yesterday
being Sunday no effort was made to
effect the change in the custody of the
young woman, who will probably be
given over to her relatives to-day.
INSISTS SHE IS SANE.
Arriving at the Russ House yester
day and discovering that her identity
and unfortunate condition had become
known here, Miss Lowry, with her. at
tendant and aunts, hurriedly moved to
the Occidental Hotel, where they re
luctantly made a statement last even
ing. Miss Lowry insists that she is
perfectly sane, but fatigued after her
two long sea voyages and still troubled
over the experiences attending her de
tention in Manila. When seen last even
ing for a moment she declared that the
charges of insanity were without foun
dation. Referring to her attempt to
shoot Easthagen. she said she had been
forced to do so because of derogatory
remarks he had made against her. Be
fore she could make a further state
ment Miss Gertich and Miss Clark in
sisted that the interview cease, and the
door of the well-guarded young woman
was closed against reporters.
Miss Gertich later admitted that she
had been detailed to accompany Miss
Lowry to San Francisco, and expected
to turn over her charge to the aunts
to-day. Continuing she said:
I am an army nurse, and, in addition to be
ing sent over as companion to Miss Lowry, I
wan officially designated as lier guardian. As
soon as I am authorized to transfer her to the
care of her relatives I will return to the Phllip
liiics Dr. Carter and other medical authori
ties that carefully observed Miss Lowry's case
in Manila agreed that, wniie their patient's
mind was unbalanced, there wan no reason
for believing that she was Incurable. Hallu
cinations seemed to be her principal trouble,
and if not carefully watched It was feared
that she might do harm to others.
NURSE EVER VIGIL.
Miss Lowry is about 27 years of aÂ«e
and not unattractive. During the
voyage over from Manila she conduct
ed herself in a rational manner and
spent the greater part of her time on
deck under the watchful eye of her
attendant. The only time yesterday
she became excited was when she real
ized that she would not be allowed to
go her own way, and when she sud
denly discovered that her troubles In
Manila were to become a matter of
public gossip here. Although the young
woman says that her reason for attack
ing Easthagen was because he had
been talking about her, another story
is that it had been reported in Manila
that he had been paying attentions to
another woman and this had aroused
Miss Lowry's resentment.
While playing with his father's rifle
yesterday Edward Johnson, a 14-year
old boy, received an ugly wound In the
hand. Not knowing that the gun was
loaded, he placed one hand over the
muzzle and pulled the trigger. The
bullet, tore through his hand. The
wound was dressed by Dr. Herzog at
the Central Emergency Hospital.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 16, 1905.
YOUTHS BOOKED
FOR BURGLARY
Edward Kennelly and Mark
Hayes Break Into a Shop
and Are Heard by Woman
Two youths, Edward Kennelly, 19
years of age, and Mark Hayes, 17, were
arrested early yesterday morning
through the presence of mind of a wom
an and by the prompt response by the
police to her call for assistance.
Shortly after 3 o'clock Mrs. Charles
Maritzen, Hayes and Laguna streets,
was awakened by a noise in the base
ment, which is occupied by her hus
band as a paint shop. She listened for a
moment and then telephoned to the
City Hall police station that burglars
were breaking into the paint shop. Ser
geant M. J. Griffin and Policemen W.
E. Dowie and F. E. Winters jumped
into a patrol wagon and were rapidly
driven to the scene of the burglary. It
was discovered that a wire screen and
a pane of glass had been removed from
a window of the paint shop and en
trance effected.
Maritzen opened the door with his
key and the officers entered. The two
young burglars were found hiding in
the shop and were promptly arrested.
It was apparent that their object had
been to enter the grocery above the
paint shop. They had utilized a step
ladder and with a saw had cut a hole
in the ceiling. 'A few minutes more
and the hole would have been
enough to enable them to crawl
through into the grocery.
They were taken to the City Prison
and charged with burglary. It is
thought that they are new to the burg
lar business, as they are not known to
the police. Their records will be looked
into this morning.

M. Sproul acting national deputy:

cilor, Mrs. L. Gould; treasurer. Mrs. Minnie

guide. Miss Florence Schetrel; inside guard.
Miss Maud S. Turman; outside guard. J. May
<Jer; junior ex-associate councilor. Miss Emma
Koopman; trustees. Miss Irene Autzen, Harry
Cove W. W. Thompson.
The ceremony was followed by an
installation supper, at which Miss C.
Jorgensen was presented with a beau
tiful emblem of the order In recogni
tion of the services she has rendered
during her twoj terms as councilor.
The officers of Martha Washington
Council of the same order will be pub
licly Installed In Protection Hall,

ter the ceremony there will be a party
and a collation. .

PRETTY GIRL
ENDS HER LIFE
Grieving Over a Love Affair,
Sarah M. Bryant Becomes
Despondent at Seventeen
Because she thought a man with ]
whom she was infatuated was angry
with her, pretty Sarah Mabel Bryant
committed suicide yesterday by drink
ing carbolic acid. Before she took
the poison the girl destroyed the
man's photograph. In her last pa
thetic note to him she used only his
initial, evidently endeavoring to carry'
her secret to the grave. The girl was
scarcely past 17 years old and w as of a
cheerful disposition until recently. She
was employed as a domestic by Mrs.
Louise Caddy of 948 Guerrero street, at
whose house she committed suicide.
Her aunt, Mrs. M. Kelley, to whom
Sarah confided the farewell note to the
man with whom she was in love, de
clared that she did not know that her
niece had any love affair. Mrs. Kelley
said she did not know the "Fred W."
to whom the dead girl referred.
The young woman returned to Mrs.
Caddy's home from a visit to her aunt
at about 10 o'clock Saturday night.
Mrs. Kelley said that she had com
plained about her health, but not of the
trouble that made her weary of the
world.
In her own room Miss Bryant pre
pared to die in a manner that showed
determination. The oversensitive girl
took "Fred's" picture and tore it to
bits. She next scratched the label from
the vial of carbolic acid and drank its
contents. Her body was discovered yes
terday morning by Mrs. Caddy. The
Coroner was notified and the remains
were taken to the home of her aunt at
2970 Twenty-fifth street.
The following pathetic note to the
man in the case was addressed to "Mr.
F. W.":
My Darling F. âWhen this note it read to you
I will be far away. In the land where all my
sins will be forgiven I know, my darling. I
know now why you were angry, but God
knows lam innocent. It's all for you. dearest.
You said you were not angry, but I know
differently. May God forgive me, for my heart
is broken. I was unfortunate. Good-by. my
love my only love. I hope that you may never
meet with my kind again, but I loved you as
true as any heart could. Think of me, some
times Fred. Farewell, my darling friend.
MABEC BRYANT.
This touching adieu was inclosed in
a note to Mrs. Kelley, which reads as
follows:
Auntie.âGive this letter I have written to F.
Bay nothing about htm; above all, don't tell
who he Is. It will go hard with him. You
know what I told you last night. It's ail true.
I loved him. only him. Good-by, dear auntie
You know where he lives.
The dead girl has borne an excellent
reputation. She was employed by Mrs.
Caddy for about eight months.
The man for whom the girl commit
ted suicide was located last night by
Deputy Coroner P. J. McCormick. He
is Frederick W. Clapp, a motorman on
the Guerrero street line. According to
his story he had only known the girl
for a few months. They had a misun
derstanding a few days ago. Clapp
says he had no idea that the girl was
Infatuated with him.
STOLE WOMAN'S POMPADOUR.âFred

port, and two friends are accused of having

says Maxe tried to steal her shoes. He was
arrested by Corporal Coulter. The other two
escaped.
MODERN CRUSOES
RETURN TO PORT
Guardians of Clipperton
Island Beach Home With
Tales of Life Among Birds
The weird tales of Jules Verne and
the history of Robinson Crusoe and
his man Friday have a modern exem
plification in the story told by the party
of three who returned yesterday from
Clipperton Island. They are Luphy
Jerry, John Sousa and Louie Fazio.
For a year they have been isolated on
that desolate spot in the broad waters
of the Pacific.
Yesterday they came back on the
tittle schooner yacht Josephine, which
flitted in through the Golden Gate at
daybreak under a cloud of snow-white
canvas. She looked more like a bird
than a vessel which had been the
home of five human beings for forty
days.
Her passengers had been throuerh all
kinds of experiences. Isolation, winds,
storms, encounters with monsters of
the deep, hair-breadth escapes from
drowning and the other perils that
those who follow the sea in frail craft
are wont to encounter.
The tale as told by Luphy Jerry is
one of thrilling interest. He said that
in company with his two companions
he sailed away from this port on the
schooner Una December 22. 1903. for
Clipperton Island to act as carekeeoers
for the Pacific Island Company of Lon
don, who own the plant at that place.
They arrived there on January 13. 1904.
The Una then returned to this port.
\ They were the only human beings left
on this desolate spot, which is 1850
miles from here and about 700 miles
due west from Acapulco.
( Their life was a strange one. The
I only sound that greeted their ears was
the flapping of the wings of millions
lof birds that inhabit that bleak island
' and the thundering of the great break
| ers as they dashed up on the coral
reefs of the shore. The great deposit
of guano on the islands Is now of in
, calculable value.
CRABS STEAL EGGS.
Immense land crabs infested the is
land, and it was no uncommon occur
rence to see a crab with a large egg in
I its claws making tracks across the tf
| land, at a great rate of speed.
The tropical weather allowed them to
! wear the scantiest of attire, and some
! times it was so hot that they even
; wandered around with only the attire
1 that nature gave them.
For six months the rain came down
lin torrents. They had plenty of can-
I ned food, but needed little of this, as
I ducks and flsh were there in great
i abundance.
They learned to become fond of the
br3ins and liver of porpoises and all
! kinds of big sea monsters. Eggs in
1 great plenty lay at the very door of
their domicile. There were all kinds of
I dainty little flsh, and the lobsters had
i a flne and delicate flavor. In the spring
| great flocks of canvas, mallards and
i teal ducks came within easy shooting
I range. They whiled away their time
! looking after the buildings there, flsh
] Â»ng, hunting and had much time for
â  reading. The plant on the islands has
cost about $50,000. No guano has been
removed from the islands since 1900,
as the property is in litigation.
Among the different varieties of
birds that swarm about the island are
sea gulls, boobies, ganets, terns and
man-of-war hawks.
THEY BEHOLD A SAIL.
No sail and no view of human life
came within the range of. their vision
during their eleven months' sojourn
there, and early in December a speck
appeared upon the horizon. They were
all frantic with joy and rushed down
to the beach, wondering what it could
be. It gradually drew nearer and* to
their amazement came straight toward
them. They had not expected to be
sent for until January, and so thought
that the little vessel had been driven
out of her course. They yelled, shouted
and made all kinds of signals. Sudden
ly the vessel dropped anchor, and a
boat came toward them, and, after a
hard tussle with the breakers, was
landed high and dry on the beach. They
then learned that it was the little
yacht Josephine that had been sent to
bring them back to San Francisco. To
fill their places as caretakers Charles
Larsen and Gus Greenberg went there
and are now on the island.
The Josephine was in command of
Captain Lindridge, with A. Freeman
as first mate. On December 7 last
the party set sail for this port. For
the first few days the weather was
fine. Suddenly a great southeast gale
sprang up, which continued for two
days and pitched the little twenty
tonner around in a lively fashion. In
the midst of the gale Seaman Freeman
was knocked overboard by the main
boom lift being carried away. He fell
almost within the reach of a great
school of sharks. Two of the most
savage of the lot made a grab at him
just as his companions succeeded in
hauling him out of the water. The
weather remained practically fair un
til last Thursday when they encoun
tered a northwest gale. One great
wave swept Louie Fazio overboard,
and had he not been an expert swim
mw he would have been drowned, as
the fury of the gale was so great that
the little yacht was almost unmanage
able. He was finally dragged on board
in an almost unconscious condition.
The Josephine was thirty-nine days
in making the voyage. She is owned
by F. W. Wakefield. As soon as she
landed here the three recluses rushed
ashore and made great haste for their
homes in Oakland, where their people
live. Their Joy at being back again
was unbounded.
NEW MANAGER OF THE
ST. FRANCIS ARRIVES
Jnmes Woods of New York Will
Enter Upon His Important
Duties To-Day.
James Woods of New York, who
was recently chosen by the directors of
the St. Francis Hotel to manage that
handsome hostelry, arrived from the
East last evening with his wife and
will enter upon his new and important
duties to-day. Manager Woods jour
ntyed westward by way of the Sun
set route, and, reaching California a
week ago, came north leisurely, stop
ping off at the more Important towns
in order to become acquainted with
the managers of the different hotels,
to whom he wag introduced by Wil
liam MfMurray, who, with Director
Thomas of, the St. FranciÃ, met Woods
in Los Angeles.
The new manager comes to San
Francisco, with some of the best rec
ommendations ever carried by an
American hotel man. Although com
paratively a young man, Manager
Woods has had valuable experience in
the hotel business, beginning in New
Orleans, where he was manager of
the St. Charles. After a varied ex
perience In other well-known Eastern
hotels he went abroad on an extended
tour, during which he gleaned much.
knowledge of the method of business
among the hotel nyn on the Eastern
continent. Returning to New York he
entered the employ of Manager Boalt
of the Waldorf-Astoria, where he later
became assistant manager. It was In
this important position that Woods
proved his well-earned reputation for
ability and it was this fact that finally
led to negotiations between himself
and the directors of the St. Francis.
In discussing * his new position last
evening he said:
This is my first visit to San Francisco, and.
in view of the fact that I have been in your
city only a few hours, it is not to be expect- .1
that I can have much to say of it. I will tuk>
up the management of the St. Francis to-mor
row. I have been cjoarly informed as to the
peculiar conditions of the hotel business In thlj
city and will carefully study out the best
means of pleasing the California public. I
hope to be successful, and will begin work as
sured of the hearty co-operation of the directors
of the hotel.
W. H. Dohrmann, the auditor of the
hotel company, who has been acting
as it manager since Allan Pollok re
tired, will return to his former dut'es.
During the short time that Dohrmann
had the management of the hotel,
though making no pretension of ex
pert knowledge of the business, he
proved a success and he retires highly
complimented by the guests of the
establishment, to whose comforts he
has catered.

PUBLICATION OFFICE THIRD AND MARKET STREETS, SAN FRANCISCO
MONDAY JANUARY 1Â«, 1905
ANOTHER FINE WEEK IN TRADE.
TRADE conditions during the second week of the new year
were fully as promising as during the first week, which; as has
been seen, was exceptionally promising. The usual midwinter
lull is much lighter than in the ordinary year, and all sections report
a very good distribution of merchandise, except some parts of the
-South, where the depression in the cotton industry, due to the
enormous crop and recent sharp decline in prices, are having a de
terrent influence, more or less pronounced, such as harder collec
tions, with some extensions requested, etc.
The manufacturing interests are sending in very good reports
at present and many branches state that they have orders booked
for several months ahead, indicating that production has not yet
overtaken consumption. The best returns come from the iron and
steel trades, though the woolen mills are actively engaged, with
stocks of raw wool at leading centers lighter than for a good many
years, while the market for raw wool rules firm at the good prices.
New England boot and shoe factories report business excellent, and
even the cotton mills expect to do a lively business when the con
dition of the raw cotton market becomes more definitely settled. An
encouraging feature in these four important industrial lines is the
' general employment of labor at good wages and the absence of large
. and troublesome strikes. In fact, taking the country as a whole, the
labor situation is more settled than for years.
Other current returns point to continued activity in general
trade. The railroad earnings continue to show a steady increase
over corresponding periods last year. The failures last week were
324, against exactly the same number in 1904, and of these the
South contributed rather more than its usual proportion, owing to
the disturbed condition of the cotton industry, on which that half
of the country so largely depends. The bank clearings for the week
made a fine showing, the gain over 1904 being 28.2 per cent, with
only two cities âNew Orleans and St. Paulâout of the first forty
.. three on the list exhibiting a decrease from last year, and these two
losses were insignificant, being .2 and 3.4 per cent, respectively. The.
aggregate clearings were $2,905,033,000, showing, a vast volume of
general trade throughout the country. The 'only lines showing
lassitude are cotton and provisions, but the tone of the latter market
seems to be slowly improving, and there is no actual depression in it.
An excellent feature of the times is the remarkably favorable
condition of farm produce. Look wherever we will, we find stocks
in farmers' hands light, while quotations for almost everything pro
duced on the farm are above the average of the past ten or fifteen
years. This is a condition which, in itself alone, is sufficient to
guarantee another year of prosperity. The railroads. t.K>. are again
running short of cars in some sections, showing a large distributive
trade in produce and merchandise, while the excellent condition of
the crops points to a large production for the coming year, as far
as any pointing can be done so early in the season.
The Xew York stock market, which is largely governed by 'he
conditions in the industries and crops, is reflecting the favoralle
outjlook by a buoyancy and bullishness which nothing can destroy.
Even the fulminations and dire predictions of the frenzied Boston
speculator, so effective a month ago, now fall flat and cause no ap
preciable effect on the market, except to provoke a laugh and a
sneer. His last announcements fell flat. It is pretty hard, when
the country is doing a vast legitimate business, with more cash
money than it really knows what to do with and fine crop prospects
assuring another good year, to make the average citizen believe
that the country is going to the dogs, so Lawson will probably
have to give it up as a bad game.
On this coast the conditions are as fine as elsewhere in the
United States. We have sold almost all of our wheat, barley, dried
.and canned fruits, hops, beans and other leading products of the
soil. Our wool # clip has all been sold and the market is bare. The
weather from a farmer's standpoint has been ideal ever since last
September and the crops all over the coast are looking finely. Our
banks are still heavily supplied with funds, c&llections are good, the
failures are certainly not over the average, real estate and building
operations, both in city and country, are extremely active, and all
conditions indicate continued prosperity.
THE COLORADO SETTLEMENT.
THE adjustment" of the gubernatorial contest in Colorado .makes
it apparent that the raw head and bloody bones news sent
out by Senator Patterson's papers was not justified. Adams,
the Democratic, and Peabody, the Republican candidate, came to
gether and made an orderly arrangement. Governor Peabody
appointed two Republican jurists to the Supreme bench. They were
sworn in and then Mr.*s\<lams was given the right of way, took the
oath as Governor, and Peabody makes his contest before the Legis
lature. Governor Peabody shows well in the whole affair. He
stood beside Adams in the inaugural ceremonies and performed his
polite and formal part in welcoming his competitor to a seat from
which he will probably oust him by a legal contest.
Governor Peabody, like Governor Steunenberg of Idaho, had
a hard duty to perform in preventing the subversion of the state
itself by the same criminal and disorderly element. Both enforced
the law. Both purged the affected communities of an element that
murdered men and destroyed property, and was in lawless alliance
with the local officiary. In doing this, both incurred censure and
were widely misrepresented. There the analogy ceases. The Demo
cratic party of Idaho put Governor Steunenberg under the ban ana
retired him from public life for being a true American and enforcing
the law. The Republican party of Colorado renominated Governor
Peabody, indorsed his administration and fought a mighty battle
to re-elect him, and no doubt did so, as will appear when the elec
tion frauds and crimes committed to defeat him are exposed by the
Legislature.
The courts have* already jailed scores of election officers and
defilers of the ballot-box. A legislative investigation was decided
upon, because there was a tacit agreement that women voters and
election officers should not be sent to jail. Yet they falsified tally
sheets and stuffed ballot-boxes with a free hand. The Legislature
can expose the frauds of the female electors without subjecting them
to penalties for felony. There may be those who object to this,
believing that a woman who corrupts the ballot-box should take pot
luck and jail with men who do the same. But let us at least lift
our hats to the gallantry of Colorado. 1

PRIMER OF LITERATURE
What is the Literature of to-day?
Fiction.
How is Fiction divided?
Into Historical Novels and Nature
Books.
What is a Historical Novel?
One that shows no trace of History
or of Novelty.
What is a Nature Book?
A volume of misinformation about
animals.
Why are Nature Books popular just
now ?
Because they are the fashion.
Mention some recent Nature Books.
' The Lions of the Lord," Pigs in
Clover," "The Octopus." "The Blue
Goose," and "The Sea Wolf."
What are thâ¢ best-selling books?
Those which sell the best people.
What is a Magazine?
A small body of Literature entirely
surrounded fey advertisements.
Why is a comic paper so called?
Because it's so funny that anybody
buys it. ,
What is a Critic? *.
A Critic is a man who writes about
the books he doesn't like.
What is poetry ?
Lines of words ending with the
same sound.
What is a Minor Poet?
A poet not yet twenty-one years of
age.
What is a Major Poet?
There isn't any.
What is a Publisher?
A man who is blamed if a book
doesn't sell, and ignored if it does.
What does a publisher mean by
Problem Novels?
All, except Kipling's and Mrs. Hum
phry Ward's.
What makes a book a phenomenal
success?
Much bad. much pad, and much ad.
âCarolyn Wells, in The Metropolitan
Magazine.
The fellow who parts his hair in the
middle isn't necessarily well balanced.

! world's diamond industry. Owing to
j the well-regulated labor union of the
; diamond cutters, the number of ap
' prentices has been kept at such a
| nice balance that the demand for
I workmen is far in excess of the sup-
I ply. and manufacturers are offering
, all kinds of inducements to attract the
j men, all the manufacturers voluntar
ily paying higher wages than was
i agreed upon at the settlement of last
spring's strike. To complicate matters
the demand for cut stones has in
, creased enormously. both in the
| United States and in Europe, and at
j the same time the supply of raw nut
! terial has fallen off.

CHICAGO UNIVERSITY
GIRL WILL TRY TO
REFORM THE POLICE
PLANS OF ONE GIRL
Reform the Chicago police
force.
Revolutionize the methods of
municipal government.
Draft a new charter for Chi
cago.
ANEW co-ed at the University of
Chicago has mapped out a stren
uous programme of reform which
will mark her advent into the
city, says the Inter Ocean. Miss Willie
Curtis is her name, and she is fresh
from an exclusive finishing school of
Beacon Hill, in Boston, with her head
full of notions which she is aching to
turn loose on the long-suffering citizens
of Chicago.
She is going to assist the "powers
that make for good" in the uplifting of
the city. She is going to frame up a
new charter for the city's government;
she is going to reverse the present City
Hall methods and move the founda
tions of Western society several inches
to the east, but most of all she is going
to reform the police force.
Dress reform, reform of manners, re
form of speech, reform of moralsâit is
all there in her busy brain, and as soon
as she can get it straightened out she
will turn it loose from the Midway in
stitution and let it spread north and
west.
Her ftrst step was to organize a de
bating team which will wage merry
war on the poor policemen and air her
views on the matter of municipal clean
liness.
Miss Curtis is also great on political
economy and has pronounced ideas
about*the new charter, but she never
wanders far from the police force, the
piece de resistance of her reform move
ment.
"The Chief of Poice should be elect
ed by tbe people, and I propose to help
bring this reform about." she said. "I
have heard, among other things, of
these poicemen cheating in examina
tions, and it ought to be stopped.
"And the new charter. Woudn't it be
lovely if we could have a hand in making
Chicago's new dress and take the re
sponsibility from poor Mayor Harri
son?"
Miss Curtis is the first woman at the
University of Chicago to gain a place
on the freshman debating team.
HOTEL FOR OHILDRBN.
\ â
England's fashionable babies are
now the proud possessors %f a hotel
of their own.
It stands in London, in Pembroke
Square, Wâ and is under the control
of the Norland Institute of Nurses. It
has been opened to provide a safe and
happy home for children whose
parents are compelled by business to
travel.
The babies' hotel has been furnished
throughout to suit the peculiar needs
of the most fastidious of its little in
mates.
There are half a dozenvsuites, each
decorated and furnished in baby style,
and consisting of a day and night nur
sery, under the charge of a trained
nurse of the Norland Institute.
The furniture in all the rooms is
lilliputian in size, says the London Ex
press. Tiny chairs and table, diminu
tive water jugs, basins and washstands
have been provided for the guests.
One suite is furnished throughout
in white enamel, others are in light
oak. They .nre known as the "Speed
well" âthe badge of the Norland
nurses â "Forget-Me-Not," "Daisy,"
"Dawn," "Peace" and "Joy" suites.
In some respects the babies' hotel
will be more exclusive than hotels for
"grown-ups." The occupants of each
suite, for instance, do not associate
with any other guests, the object be
ing to test the ability of the student
nurse to amuse her little charges with
out outside help.
Guests may be of any age from 1
month to 8 years, and they are taken
by the week, month or year. Babies
who arrive before the feeding bottle
stage is over pay from 12s to Â£2 2s a
week, according to the suite. Other
children pay from Â£38 to Â£100 per an
num.

REAL LIFE CAMILLE
IMPOSSIBLE
DOROTHY FENIMORE
SOME one has written to ask what
I think of the celebrated Lady of
the Camelias, and has requested a
reply to the specific question: "Do
you think a Camille in real life
could love as unselfishly as the heroine
of Dumas' novel?"
The theme of both the novel and
drama of this name is, as you know,
the awakening of a soul under the in
fluence of love. The idea is worked out
in a manner thoroughly French, and
this fact constitutes my answer to the
question which has been asked me. Ac
cording to the testimony of many
French writers, a Camille is possible In
the social life of France.
In America, however, the woman's
character as it is portrayed would not j
appear consistent. Public opinion in ,
regard to womanliness and genuine '
love is high among us, and we have, in I
spite of our reputation for prudery, a |
blunt fashion of calling vice by its j
right lame. You will go far in our j
civilization to find any one who believes J
in sober sincerity that a pure love can j
spring from an impure life, as a beau- j
tiful flower springs from mold.

Electricity as an Anaesthetic:
Dr. L.educ of the Faculte de Medecine
in Paris has found a way of utilizing
a current of electricity to produce in
sensibility in place of chloroform or
ether. After experimenting on animals
he submitted himself to the process,
and was entirely insensible for ten
minutes, and, after his experience, was
elated rather than depressed, which is
not usually the case after the use of
drugs for this purpose.
To win in this world you must have
more confidence in yourself than you
really cmnunt to.
THE TIME TO LIVE.
Mrs. TelletâMy husband died owing
nobody.
Mr. Wise (absently)â That's an aw
ful bad time to pick out to die.
Dumas makes a straight bid for the
favor of the gallery. He throws the
unselfishness of Camllle's love into high
relief by means of her tragic death.
But did Camille die of love? Was it
not passion that killed her? In Prance
the two terms are synonymous. But
in the United States we have erected
a barrier between these separate ideas.
It Is my. private opinion that an
American Camille who had the courage
to make a sacrifice of herself for the
sake of her lover's welfare would hayÂ»
the stamina to carry the affair off
better. She would not spoil it all by
dying for love. She would try, with
the new light that had come to her, to
turn her hard good ac
count.
I do not think that It is worth our
while to keep before our eyes In our lit
erature and drama a degenerate stand
ard of love. Are our palates so Jaded al
ready that we need ginger to stimulate
them? We talk enough about the sim
ple life, andâmetaphoricallyâwe kiss
upon both cheeks the apostles of plain
living who come from foreign shores to
look us over as possible literary ma
terial. *
And yet when we have our choice be
tween common and bread-and-butter
sandwiches with our afternoon tea and
sandwiches that have anchovies or pep
pers between their cov<b~s, we pick the
latter off the plate with a daintiness of
gesture that contrasts comically with
the eagerness we show in gobbling
them down.
Why not keep before us our best
standards of love and our highest es
timate of womanliness? The first use
of an education is that it puts us in
touch with the world's finest thought.
The power which women hold in our
land gives them the privilege of realiz
ing the true dignity of woman, and her
noblest relation with man.
What's the sense-of going out of your
way to walk through mud Just because
you have your rubbers on? You may
find to your sorrow that your footgear
is not as well made as it looks, that it
leaks at a seam. If you can't avoid
puddles, it is sensible, say I, to Jump
them.
"True love's the gift which God hath
given
To man alone beneath the heaven;
It is not fantasy's hot fire.
Whose wishes, soon as granted, fly;
It liveth not in fierce desire;
With dead desire it doth not die;
It is the secret sympathy,
The silver link, the silver tie,
Which heart to heart, and mind to
mind,
In body and in soul can bind."

Biennial Convention Planned
The State Federation of Women's
Clubs at Los Angeles is now only two
tJiort weeks away, and to a disinter
ested observer it promises to look
somewhat as the Legislature did before
it.proclaimed a Senator.
Politics.' Well, it, does seem that
way, although the word really doesn't
look nice in print, does it?
One of the bombs that are due to ex
plode on. the floor of the convention is
an amendment to the federation's con
stitution providing for a biennial State
convention instead of the annual
gathering that now obtains. There
isn't a delegate going south but has
â¢her mind made up" upon that pointâ
and when a club delegate "makes up
her mind," now, something's going to
happen.
The reason offered for the change is
said to be a desire to make the gath
erings doubly strong by having them
occur less frequently. On the face of
it, that sounds not illogical. But there
are hosts of clubwomen hereabouts
who proclaim aloud that the State or
ganization is not old enough nor
healthy enough in the four years of its
being to be left unnourished and un
cafed for tor so long a period. "It will
die of inanition," they cry, even as
would an infant left without nourish
ment and care for a long period. And
then comes the question, "Why? Isn't
the. federation doing well on its present
annual basis, fed by the live current
of constant activity?"
Ah! good friends; herein are women
displaying their splendid aptitude for
the game of politics. Of course, the
â tory is not new. Napoleon knew, and
Boulanger knew, and Crispi knew the
political texture of some women for
politics, and played the game with
them. But America has not yet felt
the direct hand of women in its great
political games. But wait! Their tal
tnts are Quickening.
Bend low your ears and I'll let you
into a little secret. â¢ 'Tis this: Ambi
tion burns high in the bosom of a
very charming ladyâa clever woman,
uid one possessed of social as well as
iKlministrutive ability. She is one of
the logical candidates for State presi
dent and here's the rubâif she draws
the prize this year, with nothing do
ing socially or politically beyond the
State limits (which is mighty dull
fun), she will not hold the high of
fice for the biennial two years hence,
and a State president in biennial year
le a famous personage at the great na
tfcnal gathering. She's entertained
elaborately, courted and feted and re
ceived with charming distinction wher
ever a woman's club exists âand
there's one to be found at almost every
i rossroad throughout the East.
Then comes the meeting of mighty
minds in feminine caucus. The favor
ite candidate must âabsolutely must â
r< present the State at the biennial.
But if she adjures the honor this year,
Â»hat guarantee has she of receiving
the nomination next year? And then
thtre came light!
"I have it!" cried a subtle settler of
solemn seances. "I have it! Let's
make the federation meet every two
yt ars and then behold our candidate
holding office for the whole sweet
time." "Fine!" cried the rest of the
i>aucusians. "Fine! Then watch us
ii troduce the two-year amendment to
the constitution."
Well, there will be a large number
of watchers! In fact the women are
thinking of asking their good friend,
Fairfax Wheelan, to go south and help

Meanwhile, however, that amend
ment must be gagged, bagged and
tlung into oblivion if the State federa

SUFFOCATED BEFORE THEY
COULD EFFECT AN ESCAPE
Facts Show New York Lawyer and
Family Were the Victims of
Accidental Fire.
NEW YORK, Jan. 15. âIt now ap
pears that William T. Mason, the law
yer, and his family, who were the vic
tims of a fire in their home early this
morning, were in reality suffocated.
The family consisted of Mason's wife
and two children âEllen, 4 years old,
and Marion, 6 months old. A servant
also lost her life. **
Although neighbors say they heard
cries of "Murder!" and "Burglars!"
the police found nothing to indicate
that the Are was not accidental. The
whole family appears to have been
suffocated. The bodies of the father
and youngest child were untouched by
the flames. The others in the house
probably were burned after they had
died.
A policeman and a neighbor who
had heard cries attempted to break
into the house, but were driven back
by the smoke.
The chkrred bodies of Mrs. Mason,
the servant and Ellen were found in
a closet under the roof scuttle,
through which they are supposed to
have attempted to escape. The bodies
of Mason and the youngest child were
in a bedroom.
IDAHO FARMER KILLED
WHILE ON HIS WAY HOME
Victim of Murderous Thieves Found
Dead in the Road by His
Brother.
MOSCOW, Idaho. Jan. 16.âKnud
Nelson, a farmer, living six miles
southeast of Moscow, was murdered
last night. Nelson had been to the
city and was returning home when the
deed was committed. When he failed
to reach home his brother went to
search for him. He found his body on
the public road. It was evident the
man had been killed by thugs bent on
robbery, as his money and valuables
were missing. Nelson's team and bug
gy were found nearby entangled in a
fence.
PERSONAL.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 16, 1905.
NEW YEAR GIVES
GREAT PROMISE
PLEASURE RULES THE SOCIAL WORLD
WHILE CUPID STILL BECKOfIS.
The new year has not yet fallen into
prosaic swing, for the aftermath of the
holiday lime is still making itself ap
parent. Clubs and homes bear marks of
the season that linger in the memory
mote deeply than any other of the ob
servances, religious or patriotic.
There seems to be no prospect of a
lull between now and Lent; the sign 3
of the times all point toward the ex
hilaration of dance S and merriment,
with a stray shaft here and there frofh
Cupid.
ENGAGEMENTS.
The engagement is announced of Miss
Annie Bruce Fyfe of Stockton and Dr.
Thomas D. Maher of the same city.
Miss Fyfe is the daughter of Joseph
Fyfe, vice president of the San Joaquin
Valley Bank and manager of the Stock
ton Lumber Company, Dr. Thomas Is a
graduate of the University of Caifornla
medical college and captain surgeon of
the National Guard of California,
Stockton, having also been resident
physician of the City and County Hos
pital. The wedding will take place in
June.

WEDDINGS.
The wedding of Miss Rose Leaves
and Ernest Paul Allen took place on
New Year's day in the First Methodist
Church of Vallejo.
Smilax, palms and flowers were pro
fusely used in decoration and a large
number of friends attended the cere
mony.
The young people are well known in
Vallejo, both being musicians. Mrs. O.
Van Every was matron of honor, O.
Van Every best man. Six ushers led
the bridal party, which included Miss
Laura Gray and Miss Alice Allen,
flower girls, and Edwin McDonald, ring
bearer, with Miss Maud Leaves and
Miss Gertrude Leaves as bridesmaids.
The bride, who entered with her father,
was gowned in white silk mull and lace
and carried Bride roses; the matron of
honor, similarly gowned, carried a
shower bouquet of maidenhair fern.
Miss C. Nesbit played the wedding
march, and the ceremony was per
formed by Rev. H. Anguin of Vallejo,
assisted by J. M. Van Every of Oak
land.
The marriage of Miss Flora Miller
and Sigmund E. Ka>ischer occurred
yesterday afternoon at the residence of
Mr. and Mrs. S. C. Greenberg, 1461
Steiner street.
Mrs. Minnie E. Hall and Edward M.
Hibbert were married last Sunday, the
Bth, at noon. The bride was attended
by her sister, Miss Margaret L. Stock,
the groom by James J. Stock. A recep
tion was held at the home of the bride,
664 Grove street, to which only rela
tives and the bridal party were bidden.
After a trip through Southern Callfor
nio Mr. and Mrs. Hibbert will reside at
420 Noe street.

CLUB PARTIES.
The Fruit and Flower Mission of San
Francisco held Its annual meeting at
681 Sutter street on January 11, when
the following officers and directors
were elected: President, Miss Almie
Wainwtight; first-vice president, Miss
Florence Siffitt; second vice president.
Miss Mirram Wallls; corresponding
secretary, Mrs. Albert H. Coppuck; re
cording secretary, Miss Corlnne Cook;
treasurer, Mrs. F. Mandelbaum; direc
tors, Mrs. W, B. Sherwood. Miss Lillian
Norton, Miss Sarah Johnson, Miss Belle
Armer, Miss Hannah Lezynsky. The
report of the mission, whose object is
to care for the sick and needy, showed
that an average of Oiirty cases a week
had been provided with necessaries and
comforts. The members of the mission
carry baskets of supplies into the poor
homes, adding words of encouragement.
On Thanksgiving two hundred baskets
were distributed, Christmas likewise
bringing cheer to the needy. The mis-
sion thanks and appreciates the cour
tesy of the press and offers gratitude
to the public for its generosfty.

LOUBET LOSES MOTHER.
Aged Parent of French President
Called hy Death.
PARIS, Jan. 15.âMme. Loubet,
mother of the President, died at Mara
sanne to-day of congestion of the
lungs. She was 82 years of age. The
President's son, Paul, and other mem
bers of the family were at her bed
side when she expired. President Lou
bet had expected to leave this morn
ing for Marasanne. but the announce
ment of the death of his mother came
before he was able to-depart.
Mme. Loubet contributed much f.o
the President's prestige, as she was a
type of the simple country class.
The funeral will take place on
Tuesday and President Loubet will
leave Paris tq-morrow in order to at
tend. Many officials and members of
the diplomatic corps have called at
the Elysee Palace to express sympa
thy with the President. ,
MONTANA PIONEER CALLED.
Simeon R. Bnford, a Prominent Demo- j
â¢ â¢rat. Passes Away.
BUTTE, Mont., Jan. 15.âA special
to the Miner from Virginia City says
that Simeon R. Buford, a pioneer and
one of the best known men in South
ern Montana, died at 6 o'clock' this
morning after a prolonged illness of
inflammatory rheumatism. Buford
drove an ox team from Canton, Mo.,
to Virginia City in 1565. For ten
years he was the chairman of the
Democratic Central Committee and j
was an influential member of the con- j
stitutional convention at the inception
of statehood. He was also State Sen- j
ator for two terms and was promi- j
nently identified with the various
lodges of the State.

FALLS AS HE TURNS ON GAS
AND IS THEN ASPHYXIATED
Specialist in Bureau of Entomology in
Washington Meets a Pecu
liar Death.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 15. âGeorge
H. Harris of Beverly, Mass., for sever
al years a specialist in the Bureau of
Entomology of the Agricultural De- J
partment, was found dead in his room
here to-day. He is believed to have j
been stunned by falling on the floor j
in an attempt to light the gas, and
an open gas jet caused his death. Har- J
ris was 45 years old.
Sleeping Car to and From Sacramento.
Sleeping car service is now afforded daily
by the Southern Pacific between San Francisco
and Sacramento. A comfortable standard
sleeper le attached to train leaving San Fran
cieco at 8:05 p. m.. returning with train leav
ing Sacramento at 4.00 a. m., the latter arriv
ing in San Franclaco at 7:50 a. m. Passen
gers to Sacramento will be allowed to occupy
their berths until 7 o'clock In the morning,
and when coming to San Francisco may take
possession of their berths any tune after 9
o'clock on the evening before, thus insuring
a refreshing night'b sleep and an early ar
rlval at either city. Â»

NEW OFFICERS
IN THE LODGES
Four Subordinates of Odjl
Fellows Honor Brothers
With Responsible Posts
REMEMBER PAST GRANDS
A Week's Work Well Done by
District Deputy M. H. Lud
low and His Associates
During the last w r eck District Deputy
Grand Master M. H. Ludlow was busy
installing the officers of the lodges of
Odd Fellows in district No. 68 of this
city, which come under his Jurisdic
tion. In this work he was ably as
sisted by his staff of acting grand of
ficers, with L. C. Mershon as grand
marshal.
The Arst officers installed were those
of Golden Gate Lodge and after the
ceremony Past Grand I. E. Jarrett, on
behalf of the lodge, presented to F. N.
Holmes, the retiring noble grand, a
handsome emblematic gold locket in
appreciation of the close attention he
paid to the lodge and the work of the
order during his term. The presenta
tion was followed by an installation
banquet, at which there were remarks
for the good of the order by the in
stalling officer, the grand marshal,
Henry W. Osthoff, J. W. Brooks, R. A.
Wheeler and George J. Strong.
The district deputy next installed
the officers of Spartan Lodge in South
San Francisco Masonic Hall. The in
stalling officers were received with a
cordial welcome and their work was
performed in a manner that reflected
great cerdit upon the deputy and those
who assisted him. Among the promi
nent members of the order present
was Grand Secretary George T. Shaw,
who is a member of the lodge. At the
close of the lodge business there were
congratulatory addresses by the grand
secretary, the district deputy and other
members of the installing team.
The same officers installed the of
ficers of Sargent Lodge for the current
term. After the proclamation of in
stallation R. R. Moody, P. G., speak
ing for the lodge, presented to the re
tiring noble grand, J. M. Starkey, a
magnificent gold jewel, indicative of
his rank in the organization; and to
this there was a feeling response.
Deputy Ludlow, who Is a/ very earnest
and loyal Odd Fellow, and his staff
were highly complimented for the ex
cellent rendition of the Installation
work. At a banquet which followed
there were addresses by the installing
officers and the new officers.
The last of the installations in the
district was that of the officers who
will rule Fidelity Lodge for the cur
rent term. This was done In a credit
able manner in the presence of a large
number of those entitled to witness the
ceremony. After congratulatory ad
dresses the retiring noble grand, W. H.
Mitchell, was presented by Past Grand
W. B. Curtis, on behalf of the lodge,
with a valuable watch charm of unique
design.
Old Man Knocked Down by Car.
While trying to cross ir\ front of a
Union-street car last night Andrew
Anderson, 76 years old, living at 220
Montgomery avenue, was knocked
down and sustained a fracture of the
skull. Anderson was going on an
errand and as he was crossing Mont
gomery avenue at Broadway he did
not notice an approaching car. Before
the feeble man could get across the
track the car knocked him down. He
was taken to the Central Emergency
Hospital for treatment. Dr. Herzog,
the attending physician, did not deem.
It advisable to operate on the man on
account of his condition, as he was
apparently dying.

PROPOSALS.
DEPARTMENT OF THE INTERIOR, WASH
INGTON, D. C, Jan. 9. 1905.âSealed pro
posals will be received at the office of the
United States Reclamation Service, 1108
Braly building, Los Angeles, Cal., until 2
o'clock p. m., March 15, 1905, tor the con
struction of the Laguna dam and sluice
ways, involving the excavation of about
282.000 cubic yards of earth, excavation of
about 300,000 cubic yards of solid rock,
placing of aboyt 305,000 cubic yards of solid
rock in the dam and masonry core walls,
building of about 27,150 cubic yards of con
crete, laying of about 80,000 square yards of
paving and furnishing and driving of about
63.000 linear feet of sheet piling, for the di
version of a part of the Colorado River
about ten miles northeast of Yuma, Ariz.
Bids will be received for the entire work.
Specifications, form of proposal and particu
lars may be obtained by application to the
Chief Engineer, United States Reclamation
Service, Washington, D. C.; to J. B. Lippln
cott. Supervising Engineer, United States
Reclamation Service, 1108 Braly building,
Los Angeles, Cal., or to Homer Hamlin,
Engineer, United Stares Reclamation Ser
vice, Yuma. Arls.. at whose offices the plans
may be Inspected. Each bid must be ac
companied by a certified check for ten thou
sand ($10,0oo) dollars, payable to the order
of the Secretary of the Interior, as a guar
anty that the bidder will, if sueccessful,
promptly execute a satisfactory contract and
furnish bond in the sum of 20 per cent of
the contract price for the faithful perform
ance of the work. The right is reserved to
reject any or all bids, to accept one part and
reject the other, and to waive technical de
fects as the Interests of the service may re
quire. Proposals must be marked "Pro
posals Laguna Dam. Yuma Project, Califor
nia." Bidders are invited to be present
when bids are opened. E. A. HITCHCOCK,
Secretary.

CHURCHES ADD TO LIBRARY. âBerkeley,
Jan. 15.âThe Federation of Churches has sent
out through its pastors suggestions to all
church goers that a missionary library be es
tablished in the new Carnegie library building
on Shattuck avenue. The library trustees have
concurred In the idea and will set apart space
for the purpose. Church goers have been asked
to contribute either a dollar or a missionary
book to a committee of which Secretary Bent
ley of the University Y. W. C A. is chairman.
ROBBED OF HIS WATCH.âJ. Susey, who
says he Is a clerk and who lives at 723 McAl
lister street, reported to the police that he had
been robbed of a gold watcn by a negro who
accosted him last night on Pacific street be
tween Kearny and Montgomery. Sussy says
the negro engaged him in conversation and
suddenly jerked the watch from his pocket and
its chain, ran toward Kearny street and disap
peared.
SANTA CLAUS MAKES FUN
ON SHIP IN MID-OCEAN.
FESTIVAL-HELD ON THE SHERMAN
Christmas Celebrated In Home Fashion bu
Soldiers Returning From Philippines.
The transport Sherman from Manila
arrived In port at daylight yesterday
morning:. Captain Bruguiere reports
that the voyage was stormy all the
way across and, while there was no
special storm of any great violence,
winds and rains were an every day
occurrence. The Sherman left Manila
December 15. Christmas was celebrat
ed on board in grand style. This hap
pened a day out from Nagasaki. At
that port a Christmas tree was pro
cured and the ladies of the ship dec
orated it out in splendid shape. Im
mediately after dinner the whole com
pany qf cabin passengers assembled in
the big dining saloon to join in the
entertainment. There were twenty
children and all were presented with
Christmas gifts by a Jovial Santa Claus,
in the person of Lieutenant Mack K.
Cunningham, signal corps. Later in
the evening there was splendid music
by a quartet of soldiers, and after
ward ell joined In drinking "Merrf
Christmas" in bumpers of sparkling
punch. New Year's eve was also cele
brated In becoming style.
â¢To add to the general social life
of the voyage a christening took place
on board on December 18. It was the
first anniversary of the birth of the
pretty little baby daughter of Captain
F. E. Foss. The ceremony took place
on Sunday immediately after morning
service. Chaplain Oliver C. Miller of
ficiated and named the little babe
Violet Grace.
An accidental shooting 'affair also
served to enliven up the trip. Two en
listed men were discussing the merits
of a carbine in a friendly manner,
when the gun was dropped to the deck,
with the result that John Sylvester was
shot through the calf of the leg.
Only one stowaway was found. He
managed to get on board at Honolulu.
Albert d'Arcy, a former employe of
the customs service at Manila, died
the second day out from that port. The
Sherman will be unloaded and reload
ed with all possible dispatch, as she
will sail on her return trip to Manila
on February 1.
She was thirty days and five hours
from Manila, twenty-two days from
Nagasaki, and seven days and fifteen
hours from Honolulu.
Captain Bruguiere reports that the
big boiler that she carried over on her
outgoing trip for the naval tug Wom
patuck was safely landed at Cavite. It
weighed forty-Aye tons. In order to
hoist it from the rrajd of the Sherman
it was necessary to shore up the decks.
There was only half an inch leaway be
tween the boiler and the hatchway.

sengers: â¢

Elder Has Terrible Trip.
The steamship George W. Elder had a tough
experience coming- down the coast from
Astoria which delayed her twenty-four hours
In reaching Bort. The Columbia River bar was
smooth when she passed out. but she had only
touched her nose into the deep when she ran
Into a heavy sea and a strong head wind.
The hurricane she ran into Just before Christ
mas, on her down trip, was practically a
summeq sea compared with the terrible storm
she encountered on this voyage. The gale was
a furious one. The seas swept her decks from
stem to stern. At times the main deck was
flooded with water a foot deep. When a little
above Cape Blanco the storm raged so furiously
that the vessel was hove to for several hours,
apd twice she was obliged to slow down. Great
mountains of water constantly poured over
her and swashed down through her ventilators.
She was sixty-six and one-half hours making
the trip, Whereas her usual time is about
forty-eight houra
Anemone Is a Success.
The trial trip of W. T. White's new launch
Anemone took place yesterday. She Is a smart
little boat and will be a great acquisition to
the craft of the bay. She Is handsomely and
luxuriously furnished and finished. Her outside
woodwork Is of teak, while her cabins are of
rich mahogany. She is 41 feet long, 8 foot
beam and her draught is 3 feet 4 inches. She
Is fitted with a standard gas engine of twenty
five horsepower and Is lighted by electricity.
In appearance she is what is known as a tor
pedo style boat. She was built by J. C.
Beetle.
Ohio to Go on Cruise.
The battleship Ohio will start on a two
weeks' cruise to-morrow. Captain Logan has
not stated the destination of his ship, but it
is expected she Is bound for the south.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
A storm of moderate energy is central off
the Oregon coast and has caused rain from
Fresno and Point Conception northward and
cloudy weather over the entire Pacific Coast.
The pressure continues -to faU over the coun
try west of the Rocky Mountains. There has
been but little change in temperature over
the Pacific slope.
Southeast storm warnings are displayed from
Point Loboc to Eureka.

INDEPENDENT ELEVEN
BEATS ALBION ROVERS
Vampires Win Close Game From
". Eagles and Occidental-Pickwick
Match Is Postponed.
The Independents beat the Albion
Rovers by a score of two goals to one
in a match for the championship of
the California Association Football
League at Freeman's Park, Golden
Gate, yesterday afternoon. On the
Webster-street grounds, Alameda, the
Vampires defeated the Eagles by the
â¢narrow margin of one goal to nothing.
The match at Idora Park, Oakland, be
tween the Pickwicks and the Occi
dentals was called off on account of a
Wet field.

RAINY WEATHER PREVENTS
INTERNATIONAL POLO GAME
Practice Contest Is Substituted and
Reds Win by a Score
of G to 2.
SAN MATEO, Jan. 15.âOwing to
bad weather the international polo
game was postponed to-day and a
match of mixed sides played instead.
The Reds were winners by a score of
6. to 2. Those wearing the crimson
colors were Sir George Prescott, Cyril
B. Tobin, R. M. Tobin and Captain de
Crespigny, the first and last named be
ing of the Hurlingham team. The
Whites were Francis J. Carolan, F. C.
G. Menzies, John Lawsoh and Walter
S. Hobart. The umpires were .J. O.
Tobin Jr. and Lawrence McCreery.
The match was played on the field of
C. W. Clark and the rain made the
ground too soft for any certainty of
skill. The two Englishmen playing
with the Reds did exceptionally good
wcrk and gave better promise of an
even match in the international games
than their three practice games here
have indicated. Dates of future games
will depend on the Weather.
. Nothing makes the woman who
woks more angry than to have her
kufband praise the appearance of the
W'jman next door, who is an idler.

SAY HILDRETH
SHOT FRIEND
Well-Known Turfman Jailed
in New Orleans as Re
sult of Orgie in a Resort
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW ORLEANS, Jan. 15.âSam
C. Hildreth, the well known turfman,
is in serious trouble here as the result
of the mysterious shooting of a man
by the name of John Moses at a resort.
Hildreth is now under arrest, charged
with shooting and wounding Moses, but
claims that he is noa> guilty. Neither
of the men will discuss the affair.
The bullet grazed Moses' head and
he was unconscious for several hours,
but wiH recover. The women in the
resort, who are the only ones that have
given the police any information, say
that Hildreth made a bet with Moses
that he could shoot off the tetter's hat
without hurting him. That he lost his
bet is evident.

START OFF WITH A RUSH
W T earers of the Winged "O" Shine in
the Opening Contests of the
Tournament.
The interclub hard handball tourna
ment opened at the Olympic Club yes
terday afternoon. There was a big
crowd present. Representatives from
the St. Ignatius, Occidental and Olym
pic .Ath letic clubs occupied seats in
the galleries and cheered the players.
Four games were played and the
Olympic Club team captured a major
ity. Henry Cory defeated Antrom of
St. Ignatius with ease by a score of
51 to 12.
George James beat T. Lydon by a
score of 51 to 45. Lydon led all the
way and had victory 1n his grasp, but
James' superior condition told and the
Olympic Club man won out.
Joe Condon outplayed Louis Levy
and won by a score of 51 to 28. Con
don's playing was ft revelation. His
tossing and general playing stamps
him as one of the best men on the
coast. Both men played fast and
showed superb condition.
George Cunningham defeated Tom
Leach by a score of 51 to 33. Cun
ningham was able to take Leach's fast
service and "killed" at will.
The games next Sunday will be
played at the Occidental Club. Each
man must play eight games and the
club capturing the greatest number of
contests will be awarded a handsome
silver cup presented by Kenneth Mel
rose. J. C. Nealon acted as referee
and John'J. Gleason and Al Hampton
were scorers.
PLEASANTON WANTS REGULAR
RACING MEET EVERY YEAR
Secretary Adams of the Local Asso
ciation Is Conferring With
State Officials.
PLEASANTON, Jan. 15. âThe peo
ple of Pleasanton are making an effort
to have a regular racing meet here
every year. The Pleasanton Racing
Association has conducted several
such events in the last few years, but
it is now proposed to make it a regu
lar event under State auspices. Sec
retary F. E. Adams has returned from
Sacramento, where he conferred with
Governor Pardee and a number of
members of the State Legislature upon
the project.
This place is becoming one of the
great horse centers of the United
States, and there are many noted ani
mals here all the year round. There
is no city In California which has bet
ter surroundings for such an event
than this place, and Secretary Adams
is doing all in his power to boom the
project.
The Duchess of Manchester, for
merly Miss Zimmerman of Cincinnati,
is spending large sums of her pet dogs,
and now has taken up the fad of
ing her pets manicured several times
a day.
