BOYS IN BLUE
REVEL IN MUD
Fort Miley Beats Cardinal
B on Checker Board That
Is the Limit for Slush
Twenty-two game young men, repre
senting the Cardinal B eleven of the
League of the Cross Cadets and the
Fort Miley team from the Presidio,
skirmished around in the mud at the
Presidio grounds yesterday afternoon
in fearless style." The soldiers proved
the better "mudders" and got away
with the game by a score of 10 to 0.
The field was absolutely the ltmit.
The players practiced eating chunks of
slush before they got into the game and
then they swallowed more. Fast work
and scientific play was out of the ques
tion. Several times a chunk of adobe
was mistaken for the ball and the ref
eree had a hard time straightening out
the tangles that resulted from these
blunders.
.The first half went along without
incident. Some of the players called
for bathing suits, while others insisted
on boats. As neither were to be had,
the kickers suffered noticeably. Fum
bles of all kinds and descriptions were
made and repeated. Fort Mlley was
the stronger in bucking the line, but
the cadets held their own.
Cardinal B's men were all in when
the second half had gone but a short
distance. The soldiers forced the ball
to B's twenty yard line and then lost it
on a fumble. The Cadets were forced
to kick, but when the ball was snapped
to Coffey he fumbled and allowed the
pig skin to pass him and go through
to the goal. One of his team mates
rushed it to the five yard line where it
was given to Fort Miley. A touchdown
for the soldiers resulted a moment
later.
From that time on the boys in blue
go* Into the game. They managed to
buck the line hard to score another
touchdown a short time before the
game came to an end. Pento, the full
back, tried to kick goal twice, but
failed because his feet were too heavy
to lift. The teams lined up as follows:

HUNTERS REFUSE TO KILL
DUCKS THAT ARE HELPLESS
Sportsmen Will Desert Richardson's
Bay Because They Don't Enjoy
Shooting Oil-Soaked Fowl.
SAN RAFAEL, Jan. 15.âThe hunt
ers who for years past have made
Richardson's Bay their retreat during
the duck season have practically aban
doned that place and- will search for
new quarters. The cause of their sud
den departure is attributed to the oily
condition of the water in the upper
bay, which has the ducks,
preventing them from flying. The
birds, mostly canvasbacks, have found
a retreat on the marshes, but are prac
tically helpless. The late southwest
winds have caused the oil which is dis
charged in the bay near Point Rich
mond to float into Richardson's Bay
instead of finding an outlet to the
ocean.
The shores of the bay were lined
with men and boys to-day and they
had no difficulty in bagging the help
less birds. Many Sausalito hunters,
who shoot for sport, have decided to
look for new quarters at once. They
say the ducks have not a chance and
as they are real sportsmen they pre
fer to look for a pond where the ducks
will get an even break. The birds not
covered with oil have gone In search
of new homes and they will probably
not return to Richardson's Bay for
many moons. The oil-covered birds,
helpless to search for food, will perish
In a few days.

Heat Food Without Fire.
In the recent news many people
h&vjs noticed one of two references
to the fact that tinned meats for the
Russian troops are prepared by a pro
cess which enables the contents of
each tin to be served hot without a
fire. This boon is obtained by having
the ordinary tins filled with food
"jacketed" In patent tins. The patent
tin contains water, together with a
chemical mixture by means of which
the water can be raised to* boiling
point in ten minutes or a quarter of
an hour. All that is necessary to do
when it is desired to heat the focd
is to puncture the top of the patent
tin, the chemical mixture being there
by forced into the water, which soon
begins to boll. âLondon Mail.
Cutting Off Boys From Tobacco.
The Spanish Cortes is about to be
come the scene of a remarkable ex
periment in social legislation. The Min
ister of the Interior has drafted a bill
absolutely prohibiting the selling of to
bacco, cigars or cigarettes to any per
son under 17 years of age. The penal
ties vary from Â£2 to Â£20, -with Im
prisonment in exceptionally bad cases.
The measure is justified as Imperative
for the arrest of racial degeneration.
Tuberculosis makes great ravages
among Spanish laboring youths, who,
for the most part, undernourished and
already enfeebled by this and over
work, aggravate matters by spending
much of their scanty wages in tobacco,
generally cigarettes.âLondon Globe v

AGES OF GREAT MEN
IN VARIOUS FIELDS
In Each Sphere Some of the Most
Famous Have Died
Early.
A great man does not always attain
a ripe old age; in fact, hardly half of
the greatest men of ancient and mod
ern times have reached the limit of asre
set by the Bible, seventy. Among
statesmen, Mirabeau was 42. Pitt 47.
Cromwell 59, Caesar 66, Richelieu 57.
Washington 67, Charlemagne 71. Fred
erick the Great "74, Disraeli 75. Augus
tus 76, Bismarck 83, Talleyrand 84.
Of the great conquerors, Alexander
the Great died at 32, Napoleon at 51.
Hannibal at 63, Themistocles at 65. Ma
rius at 71, Marlborough at 72. Tilley at
72, Blucher at 76, Bernadotte at 80.
Wellington at 83, Xenophon at 86.
Moltke at 91.
The age of the deceased philosophers
was: Spinoza 44? Descartes 53, Hesrel
61, Aristotle 62, Socrates 68. Liebnitz 77,
Linnaeus 70, Copernicus 70. Galileo 78.
Kant 79, Plato 82, Newton 84. Hum
boldt 80.
The longevity of great writers and
poets is as follows: Byron 36. Schiller
45, Moliere 61, Virgil 51. Shakespeare
62, Dante 56, Dickens 57, Horace 57. Ra
cine 59, Scott 61, Milton 65. Cervantes
68, Aeschylus 69, Rabelais 70. Petrarch
70, Euripides 74, Cornelle 78. Victor
Hugo 83, Goethe 83, Voltaire 84, Soph
ocles 90.
To painters death came at the asres
stated: Raphael 37. Corejo 40, Van Dyke
40, Holbein 57, Velasquez 61, Rembrandt
63, Rubens 61, Michael Angelo 81. Ti
tian 99.
Musicians died at these ages: Schu
bert 31, Mozart 35, Mendelssohn 38.
Chopin 39, Weber 39, Schumann 41,
Beethoven 66, Bach 65, Palestrina 70.
Spord 75, Handel 75, Haydn 77.
And four great religious leaders died
at these ages: Calvin at 54. Moham
med at 62, Luther at 66, Confucius at
71. âExchange.
New Kind of Potato.
A new species of white potato is be
ing cultivated In France from a pe
culiar variety found in Uruguay. It is
believed that it is destined to replace
even the Irish potato. Originally a
very bitter tuber, the new vegetable
becomes, after three or four years of
cultivation, an admirable food product.
Its yield Is enormous and it is exempt
from maladies that attack the ordinary
potato. It grows best in moist soil, its
native habitat being the marshy shores
of the river Mercedes, in Uruguay. Its
flowers have a jasmine-like odor and
a delicate perfume has already been
extracted from them. After one plant
ing the species perpetuates itself from
the broken roots left in the ground.â
London Globe.
Had to Humor Customers.
It is not only cigars that vary in
name without varying in quality. The
present writer was once told when
hesitating between "fresh eggs" at
ten a shilling, and "good breakfast
eggs" at eight a shilling, or something
to that effect, that these two brands
were precisely the same, but had to be
sold as two qualities because no custom
ers would buy eggs at a shop where only
one quality was advertised. That may
or may not be so. The fact remains
that there are many qualities of eggs
known to all housewives before we
get down to what a late comedian
termed briefly "eggs."âLondon Chroni
cle.
NO MORE HARES
FOR THE DOGS
Famine of Jacks Prevents
Two Stakes From Being
Completed at Ingleside
LARGE CROWD PRESENT
Trials Furnish Some Good
Sport and Wagering Is
Lively Throughout the Day
Ingleside Coursing Park reopened
yesterday after being closed for several
weeks. Two stakes were on the pro
gramme, but they were left unfin
ished on account of a shortage of
hares, though it is hardly like*ly any
mere courses would have been run as
darkness was setting in. Haphazard,
Sacramento Boy and Reckless Acrobat
were left in the reserve stake and they
will contest for final honors next Sun
day. Quita, Carlow Boy and Rocked
Asleep were the dogs left in the class
event. Quita will meet either of the
former two dogs, as she has run her
course in the third round.
Though the day was not pleasant a
large crowd was in attendance. The
betting was unusually brisk. Short
ends scored frequently during the first
round of the reserve event, but during
the afternoon the favorite layers had
things their own way. The few out
siders that scored were held at small
odds.
Old Sacramento *3oy still possesses
the speed and working ability to de
feat the best of them. From the way
ho ran yesterday he will add a few
more brackets to his credit before go
ing into retirement. Carlow Boy
showed the most speed in the cham
pion event and will probably get first
money next Sunday. Following are
the day's results, with Judge John

Wonders of Telegraphone.
Dr. Z. B. Babbitt of New York, lec
tured before the Franklin Institute last
night upon the telegrapnone, an ap
paratus invented by Valdemar Paul
son of Copenhagen, by which the
human voice is recorded and stored on
a wire or metal disc without indenta
tion or mark of any kind, and may sub
sequently be reproduced audibly
through the telephone. The machine,
as explained by Dr. Babbitt, has the
ability to perform Aye different func
tions, as follows: Receives dictation,
reproduces to the dictator, reproduces
to one at a distant point, the machine
being under control at that point, and
records conversations between two
persons over the telephone lines with
out in any way interfering with the
operation of those lines.
It also records automatically, in the
absence of the subscriber from the
office, messages coming over "the line
during such absence, the machine
being under absolute automatic con
trol, the ringing of the bell starting
the machine, which sends a signal over
the line to the person talking, notifying
him that it is running.âPhiladelphia
Record.
Business in Butte.
Not one of the 13,000 miners of Butte
earns less than $3.50 a day, and they all
spend their money like prodigal sons.
In the old open gambling days, not long
gone by, drill-drivers wearing flannel
shirts have bet stacks of silver and
gold slugs with the recklessness of
royal rakes at Monte Carlo.
The people are all plungers. Two
men came up to a cigar case. "Let's
shake the box," said one.
As they lit their cigars the other
asked:
"How much do you want for that
mine, anyway?"
"Thirty-five thousand," was the an
swer.
"Give you 25," said the other.
"Won't take 25," replied his friend,
"but I'll just shake dice whether you
give me 25,000 or 50."
"All right. First flop or horses?"

How to Banish Fog.
The fact is incontrovertible that the
dismal fogs, which do so much to make
London a city of Dis at this season
of the year, are perfectly controllable
phenomena. They could be banished
by our taking thoughtâthat is, by for
bidding factories and the like to emit
black smoke and by inducing the or
dinary householder to adopt smoke
consuming grates. Yet In spite of all
that they suffer, the London public are
content to leave agitation for reform
entirely in the hands of a small so
ciety.âBlack and White.
MARIGOLD RUNS
TRUE TO FORM
Peggy Williams Comes In
Second in a Match Event
Between Spirited Ponies
SOCIETY FOLKS PLUNGE
More Than $5000 Changes
Hands When Francis J.
Carolan's Racer Is Beaten
Special Dispatch to Tbo Call.
SAN MATEO, Jan. 15.âAbout $600t
changed hands here to-day on a run
ning race between the favorite ponies
Marigold, owned by Walter S. Hobart.
and Francis J. Carolan's Peggy Wil
liams, in an exciting match that was
run on Carolan's private track at
Crossways farm at noon. Marigold
won easily from Peggy Williams. The
purse was $1000, put up by the owners.
Many side bets were made by specta
tors.
The distance run was one-quarter of
a mile and Marigold went the rout* in
24 seconds, beating the best pony rec
ord on the San Mateo tracks and prob
ably the State by at least one second.
Peggy Yvilliams was sired by Ho
bart's Silver Dick, out of Fanny Wil
liams, who won against Spreckels'
favorite, Becky, In the races on Janu
ary 7. Carolan rode the winner in that
match and became so attached to the
ponies that he purchased both for
$2000, so it is said, on condition that
Peggy beat any pony in San Mateo.
Otherwise L>. C. Williams, the owner,
was to accept two-thirds of that valu
ation, and it is said that he also shared
equally with Carolan the $1000 bet to
day.
Williams' son, a boy of about 16
years, rode Peggy, while the veteran
Kelly piloted Marigold.
Peggy acted badly before the start
and was somewhat exhausted, - "Ho
barfs luck" with other things stayed
by to give him the race by more than
four lengths. Hobart and Carolan
were the judges, while Paul Clagstono
started. Many society people wit
nessed the match, which was run in a
heavy shower.
The Artificial Diamond.
The reward of the inventor who can
produce artificial diamonds is so
tempting that the Moisson experiments
with the electric furnace, which were
inaugurated some eight or ten years
ago, have been continued until the
present day. They are now being car
ried on in the laboratory of the Sor
bonne, in Paris, with an ever-increas
ing success. The first diamonds made
by the electric furnace were of mi
croscopic size and few in number. In
fact, they were so minute and uncom
mon that it took a great deal of re
peated experimenting to secure
enough specimens to demonstrate be
yond a doubt that the brilliant crys
tals were actual diamonds. As the
work continued various modifications
were worked out, as the experience of
the investigators became greater, un
til now success seems imminent. The
crystals are positively known to par
take of all the characteristics of the
diamond in crystalline structure, hard
ness and chemical composition, as
demonstrated by combustion experi
ments. The largest crystal yet ob
tained is only one-half a millimeter
in length, and while this is only a
spark, it indicates that the process is
capable of yielding diamonds of good
quality, and that some day in the not
distant future the laboratory pro
cesses of Mother Nature, as exempli
fied in the Rand, may be duplicated in
man's laboratory and in a commercial
way.âNew York Times.
Birth of the Moon.
Year by year the moon is getting a
few inches more distant, and reversing
the argument, year by year in the great
past the moon was nearer to us. Pro
fessor George Darwin has shown that
long, long ago the moon revolved close
to the earth, and still earlier formed
part of this globe. From that time to
the present he calculates that at least
54,000,000 years must have elapsed. The
birth of the moon took place, therefore,
somewhere about that date in the past.
Sir Robert Ball observed that when
the moon was near to us its attraction,
must have produced enormous tides,
many times greater than those that
wash our shores to-day, and he sug
gested that these tides, by their power
ful erosions and wasting of the land,
accelerated the geological forces and
so reduced the tremendous periods
which the geologists have demanded.â
London Telegraph.
Inspectors Who Do Not Inspect.
The burning of another New York
steamboat a few days ago developed
the fact that, notwithstanding all the
vigor of Inspection after the event
manifested following the Sloeum dis
aster, the burned boat and a sister ves
sel had "escaped" the general re inspec
tion ordered by Secretary Cortelyou
some months ago. The frequency with
which vessels that "escape" Inspection
burn up seems to convey no lesson to
those responsible. If there is one branch
of the public service above others
which should be trustworthy it is that
having charge of the protection of life.
Yet experience has shown it to be par
ticularly lax. It is time it was mads
plain that profuseness of promise after
a disaster will not be taken as an ex
cuse for lack of performance in pro
venting the accidents.âPitsburg Dis
patch.

FUGITIVE SAFE
BEHIND BARS
A. A. Kratz Is Brought From
Mexico by Detective T. B.
Gibson and Lodged in Jail
HIS BONDS FORFEITED
Asserts It Is Nobody's Busi
ness Why He Was Report
ed Drowned at Long Beach
A. A. Kratz is safe behind the bars at j
the City Prison. Detective T. B. Gibson
arrived from Mexico with his prisoner
yesterday morning. Kratz wore an
"Oregon boot" to prevent him from
making a break for liberty. Gibson
looked haggard and careworn, owing to
anxiety about his prisoner and the poor
accommodation and food provided for
him during the time he was waiting
the slow process of the law regarding
Kratz's extradition. Gibson left here
on October 28 and took his departure
from Cananea, Mexico, last Friday.
During the trip from Mexico Kratz
was so ill that a physician had to be
piocured to prescribe for him. Kratz
denies that he attempted to escape
from jail at Cananea, but Gibson says
he did and that was why an "Oregon
boot" was demanded. Regarding his
reported drowning at Long Beach last
September, Kratz says it is nobody's
business. He professes to be anxious
for his trial and says he has nothing
to fear.
Kratz's brother, William, was vice
president of the Dinandt-Kratz Com
pany, commission merchants, and in
February last William collected over
$16,000 and tied with the money. He
was arrested in Kansas City, where he
Was living under an assumed name.
He was tried in Judge Dunne's court
for felony embezzlement, convicted and
sentenced to serve five years in San
Quentin. The case is on appeal to the
Supreme Court.
A. A. Kratz was. also arrested for
felony embezzlement as he was with
his brother at the time of the disap
pearance. While waiting trial in Judge
Dunne's court it was reported that he
had been drowned at Long Beach. The
report was not believed, and Judge
Dunne forfeited his bonds of $10,000 and
issued a bench warrant "or his arrest.
He was arrested in Cananea, Mexico,
on October 27. His sureties were his
father, sister and Attorney H. D New
house. The case is on Judge Dunne's
calendar for Saturday.

HOLD MEMORIAL SERVICE
FOR LAMENTED RECTOR
Honor Paid Memory of the Late Rev.
Edgar J. Lion by Large
Congregation.
A memorial service was held yester
day morning at St. Stephen's Church
for the Rev. Edgar J. Lion, who, for
twenty-eight years, had been the faith
ful rector. of the church and one of
the best known clergymen in the city.
In spite of the rain there was a very
large congregation.
Mrs. Lion was not able to be pres
ent owing to the Illness of her daugh
ter, but the family was represented by
Mr. and Mrs. Montgomery Haslett and
Edgar and Williford Lion, the two
young sons of the late rector.
The service was conducted by the
Rev. Ernest Bradley, who was also the
preacher and celebrant. Mr. Bradley
made a touching reference to the life
work of the late Mf. Lion. Hymns
sacred to his memory were sung by
the choir and congregation.
An unusually large number partook
of the holy communion and the two
altars were a mass of beautiful
flowers.

themselves amenable to the divine law.

Tuesday, Thursday and Saturday even
ings.

the board of regents, are university
advisers. Mr. J. J. Rhea, '07, teaches
bookkeeping and penmanship, and
Miss Mabel E. Palmer, '07, the direc
tor of the school, teaches stenography
and typewriting. During this term
Instruction will be given in the use
of a Burroughs adding machine. Mr.
Rhea will give advanced Instruction
in bookeeping, in addition to the lines
of work carried on during last sem
ester.

proval of President Wheeler, and let

it knows things it is "Cavalleria Rusti- i
cana," that we had direct from Mas- ,
cagni himself. Everybody was at the \
Tivoli last night to hear Pollacco's j
version, in spite of a rainstorm much
heavier than Mr. Rossini's in the third i
act of "The Barber." Signor Pollacco
has some claims to a knowledge of
Mascagni. He conducted the premiere
of "Ratcliff" in Milan in such fashion
as to win the august approval of its
maker. The "Cavalleria" is, further, a
favorite work with (the conductor, to
whom Mascagni is the most original
and prominent figure in the modern
Italian school.
At the outset one notes throughout
slightly quicker tempi than Mascagni's
own; some cuts here and there âfrom
the composer we had the opera in its
entiretyâand certain differences of
light and shade. One remembers, for
example, the scene after the rise of
the curtain, a picture of the dawn,
given in veiled, mysterious fashion by
the composerâas ominous of the day
to follow. Mr. Pollacco's dawn is a
forthright, sunny affair âprobably all
down in the score. Mr. Mascagni did
not have to bother about scores. But
Mascagni would have been happy in
the interpretation as a whole. It was
a reading admirably dramatic and
spirited and notably sensitive and the
musicians were excellently In hand for ,
the performance.
The orchestra in both the "Cay- ;
alleria" and "I'Pagliacci" was in par
ticularly fine fettle and the .conductor
altogether newly discovered as a leader
of large resource and admirable mu
sicianship.
Berlendi was the Santuzza, with her
dimpled face surrounded by all sorts
of competitive shades âchiefly that of
the flaming-eyed Santuzza of Calve.
Tc say that Berlendi's comes next to
that astounding creation is not light
praise. In local annals that is, however,
its place. It was a Berlendi night last
night. The singer grows on acquaint
ance arid bids fair to become the favor
ite here that Mexico acclaims her. Her .
Santuzza was vividly dramatic, with a
note of convincing sincerity. It was
full of the peasant feeling, from the
simplicity of the dress to the least ges- j
ture. Slgnorina Berlendi was unfor- |
tunate in her Mamma Lucia âthey cvi- |
denUy thought so on the programme,
as they omitted to mention her name.
It might have been the toothache that
Santuzza was bewailing instead of
the perfidy of Turiddu from the
Mamma Lucia's face. Berlendi sang
at her bravely and brilliantly, and so
all through.
Colli, the last of the tenors, made
his debut last night. Last but not least, j
Signor Colli seems a little to believe ;
his Turiddu an anxious, busy, import- j
ant person. He seemed very pleased
With his receptionâgot up after he died
to acknowledge the applause. But
Signor Colli does not obliterate Agos
tinl's memory. He pleases, however,
sings well, in a high, bright voice, and
looks well. Romboli was the Alflo and
sufficed and Miss Eugenic Barker of
the Tivoli forces was a vocally pleasing
Lola, short, however, on the deviltry of
the part.
"I'Pagliacci," as usual, followed, I
with an excellent performance. Ber- ]
lendl added to her laurels as Nedda,
the coquetry of the character receiv- i
ing delightful exposition at her hands, ;
her vocal assumption perhaps the best \
in local history. Frosini has-a tremen
dOUS voice. He was the Canio of the
cast and eloquent and convincing j
from all sides. He has a bad habit of
depending on the prompter, who is, in- ,
deed, a too-busy person all round, j
Signor Romboli was the Tonio, and a
capital one. He sang his prologue in
such fashion as to capture a tumultu
ous encore, and repeated it. Our old
friend Signor Cortesi sang part j
of Silvio, and. as always, competently, j
He substituted for Signor Rossi.

The Orpheum last night varied its I
programme for the week by introducing
a new sketch by Cressy entitled "The:
New Depot." Cressy and Blanche
Dayne were recipients of the usual
praises for their work, and Cressy in
particular for his genius for adapta- !
tion. Fitzgerald Introduced a new act, I
"The Mississippi Mall," which gave him
ample opportunity to show his wonder
ful quick-change work. He represented
seven distinct characters and did jus
tice to all. Chasslno repeated his work
of last week and was well received.
Eleanor Falke, with the assistance of
the orchestra, caught the house, at
least a fair proportion of it, and Clif
ford and Burke were really clever as
eccentric comedians. Clifford sings "1
May Be Crazy, But I Ain't No Fool"
almost as weil as Williams, and was
encored several times. Burke is a good
dancer and won his share of applause.
"The Ten Nelsons" deserve the reputa
tion of being "the greatest acrobatic
family before the public." for their
number at least gives them an advan
tage which some exceedingly well ex- 1
ecuted work subjected to the test of
comparison would not award them. But
It must be said that the Nelsons are
hard to duplicate and that they are de
serving of the liberal recognition that
the great audience accorded them.

111 to 121 Post Street
production at the California Theater
last night it could not have been re
ceived with heartier welcome. Joseph
Murphy, the neat, clever, witty Irish
comedian, likewise came in for a recep
tion which proved that even in these
days folks do love a truly brilliant
actor, though he has been before them
for ever so many years.
So genteel and nimble and agile was
the comedian that the part of Dan
O'Hara showed up as youthful as
though Murphy were in his teens. In
the love scenes, in pathos, and In num
erous moments the actor developed his
consumate art and the audience was
quirk to reward him with evidences of
its appreciation.
Murphy's company is capable. Each
can do something that is interesting,
and among these clever people Miss
Mary Fermier merits the highest com
mendation for her capable presentation
of the sweet Irish character, Nora
Drew. Miss Annie Mabel O'Brien's Lit
tle Jack the jockey, was prominent
and a bright song and dance won for
her an imperative encore.
out of compliment to Joseph Murphy
members of the Gaelic Dancing Club,
consisting of the Misses Bessie and
Hazel Allen and Joseph Kelleher and
John O'Connell gave a four hand Irish
jig and four hand reel.

A large audience gathered at the Al
hambra yesterday afternoon to wear
and take leave of Gadskl, who gave
her final recital of tills season. The
programme was thoroughly varied and
characteristic, ranging from Mozartâ
with two delicious examples, "Das
Veilchen" and "Schlaf mem Prinz
chen"âto Wagner, with songs by
Franz, Schumann, Schubert, Jensen,
Brahms, Bohm. Taubert, Strauss, and
a Verdi aria from "Aida." The singer
was in excellent form and was ap
plauded to the echo. After the Wag
ner group, that cpnsisted of "Dich
Teme Halle," "Gebet der Elisabeth"
and the "Llebestod" from "Tristan
and Isolde," the singer gave as en
core the "Cry of the Walkyries,
arousing a tumult of enthusiasm. The
Gadski season has been one of the>
most successful of the song recital
seasons and the singer will be most
heartily welcomed if she ever returns
to us. ...
"The Silver Slipper," the musical
burlesque by Leslie Stuart and Owen
Hail, began a two weeks' engagement
yesterday at the Grand Opera House
to two large audiences. The-spectacle
is beautifully staged, the handsome
women are elegantly gowned, and the
speaking parts of the fairly entertain
ing plot are given to care of excellent
players.
The principal and character comedy
part of the play is taken by Snltz Ed
wards, whose ability in this line is well
known. He is indescribably funny In
looks, words and gestures as Henny
Hencks, the showman, and was
heartily welcomed at his every appear
ance.
Wrenne, played by Beatrice Golden,
is a winning part played by a winning
actress, her singing of several "moon
shine" songs being artistically done.
Lora Lieb is attractive as Stella and
makes the most of a poor part.
The work of the chorus In soecial
dances and ensembles was fine, and the
rendering of the "champagne dance"
Is probably the finest ever given in San
Francisco.
Daughters of St. George.
The members of Empress Victoria
Lodge of the Daughters of St- George
have selected a committee to arrange
for an apron and neck tie party and
dance to be given in its lodge room in
the Red Men's building on the evening
of Monday, January 23.
Reminding a husband that he once
said he loved his wife is as irritat'ng
as to remind anyone of a broken New
"Vtar resolution.
Printers' Aid Society Meets.
The Union Printers' Mutual Aid So
ciety held its semi-annual meeting
yesterday afternoon. The reports of
the officers" show a great gain in the
strength of the association, with $7750
in the treasury, a gain of $1465 for
the year; $770 sick benefits were paid
and 125 members are on the roll. The
following are the officers: President.
J. Collins; vice presidents, C. E. Fisk
and O. J. Treat; secretaries. J. Paul
Spencer and G. H. Branch; treasurer,
H. V. Riff el; marshal. J. D. Laing;
guardian, L. Michelson; physician.
Avery Barnes McGIll; druggist, Her
bert F. Dugan; directors, J. W. Kelly.
J. A. Snell, R. S. Daly, J. Collins and
Robert Sleeth.
Hold Annual Election.
The St. Joseph's Benevolent Asso
ciation held its annual election of offi
cers yesterday at Old St. Mary's Ca
thedral. The following officers were
elected: William Murasky, president:
Martin O'Brien, vice president; Nich
olas Wynne, treasurer, and Michael
Dolan, recording secretary. The fol
lowing were named as councilmen:
Nicholas Wynne, Emanuel Goncahrs,
Peter Keenan and A. F. Bernard. Dr.
Jerome Hughes was appointed physi
cian for the society. The office of
financial secretary has not yet been
filled owing to the refusal of P. Mc-
Ginn to accept the position.
